RATING: 8
I really like this game. The idea is ingenious. However, though I don't think it's nearly as broken as others might claim--I've never experienced any of this so-called "Halifax Hammer" business first hand--it does seem like it might not be so perfectly balanced. Mainly, if the British player decides to ramp up his or her military and go for the throat, the French player is forced into the role of simply matching the Brits and keeping the opposing player from winning; instead of playing to win, the French player is playing to avoid losing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The cover sold me on this. I love the artwork in this game and the feeling of the era that is so well-communicated just through the art design choices. The asymmetry of the British and the French and the variety of win conditions bring a depth to the proceedings that is palpable. A reimagined use of Dominion's deck-building mechanic brings the war genre a built-in system of fatigue and supply.

The design allows for quick and decisive victories or a long, drawn out tug-of-war, depending on smart card play and timely use of natives. The rulebook is slightly obtuse regarding some of the battle/raid mechanics, but once understood, the battles are simple to understand and the raid/ambush abilities become second-nature. 

Kudos to Martin Wallace for developing such a streamlined and simple deck-building game with historical weight and feel, that also is easy to digest and simple to play. Once again, this is a deceptively deep game, one that boils down to the players' decisions and not the luck of the draw. Now I just need to try out Wilderness War to see which I like better.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Rating based on 15 plays of the 2nd edition so far.
Components [8/10] - Decent box, good artwork on the board and cards, nice mounted board.  Wood blocks and discs are adequate.  Player aids are nice and very useful.  No victory point track was included but a very nice one is downloadable from BGG.  Some small additions - villages and towns from Settlers, miniature red/blue flags for siege markers, and fortress markers from Customeeple really add to the feel of the game and would have been a nice standard upgrade rather than just having some of these things available in the limited edition.
Cost [10/10] - Reasonable for what you get.
History/Theme [18/20] - Nice historical notes are included in the rulebook and the asymmetry of the way the two sides play add nicely to the theme of the game.  A little historical flavor text added to the bottom of the cards would have been a nice feature.
Strategy [10/10] - Good strategy component to this game.  Really makes you consider tradeoffs in what to play.
Fun Factor [10/10] - Excellent, fun, tense game.  Seems well balanced.
Replayability [10/10] - Many different options to try and the card variability keeps games from being identical.  Yes, there supposedly ways to "beat" the game but we haven't stumbled across them yet and don't intend to turn it into a math exercise.
Length [10/10] - Likely 1-1.25 hours which seems very good.
Intangibles [19/20] - Very fun and satisfying game.  One of those games you walk away from thinking what you want to do different next time.  Rules are excellent - very few questions with most FAQs from the 1st edition seemingly incorporated in the 2nd edition rules.  Not a perfect 20 because sometimes the game ending (based on captured villages/towns or all towns being on the board) seems a little anticlimatic.

Overall: [95/100] = 95.0%

RATING: 8
Absolutely outstanding! So unique and original that is difficult to describe it if you didn't play it. It is a strategy game, a deck building game, a wargame, an economy game.... it is a perfect mixture of different ingredients. It is important to familiarize with each side cards, since French and British play with very different strategies. All in all, a must to have! This game is the final proof (but there was no need) that M.Wallace is truly the most visionary and original game designer that's around right now. When a game is easy to understand but difficult to play in terms of mental effort it means that you are dealing with a masterpiece. And A Few Acres of Snow is undoubtedly one.

RATING: 6
2(2)

This is a solid game. I am not remotely a fan of Dominion, but this makes the drafting gameplay interesting. You have different decks (sort of) to draft from, and both sides play differently (I've only played the French) and there are actual strategic, interactive implications to where you go and what cards you introduce to the deck. It's tense. I'm still figuring out the balance, and wonder if it's as well balanced as Twilight Struggle (which it feels somewhat similar to), but it's faster, shorter, and a lot of fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a nice two player card driven war game that is not particularly accessible on first play, which leads me to a mixed feeling of either wanting to play it again so I can better understand all the moving parts, or, never wanting to play it again, because it was a annoying gaming experience. 

I like the principal attack mechanic in the game, the siege mechanic.  I like the cubes and disks, and  upgrading from cubes to disks to double your VP's.  I like the fortification mechanic. I like the reserve deck mechanic, and the asymmetric decks are interesting.  The deck building and randomized card draws and capabilities each turn, work reasonably well.

I am not that crazy about the ambush and raid mechanics.  They feel like exercises in "guess what card is in your opponent's hand," which is not my favorite way to spend time in a game.  

And while the map appeared very attractive on its surface, the connectivity lines on the map were unexpectedly confusing for me on first play.  I spent too much time trying to "see" the difference between a road, a waterway, and an indian trail.

Overall, I can see why this game has a lot of fans on BGG where Twilight Struggle, Memoir 44, and Command & Colors, all card driven war games, dazzle a core audience of this genre, but for me, this game is merely moderately interesting.  I like it a bit, but I do not love it.

RATING: 5
My favorite M.Wallace game, but that's not saying much.... I suspect that to play this well you'll need an overall strategic plan, as well as seat-of-the-pants tactics to deal with a) the order in which your cards appear, and b) what your opponent is doing. Elegant, uncomplicated rules, despite the many actions available. Theme does not feel pasted-on. Tension comes from sieges, ambushes and raids. However, there seems to be a "correct" way to play for each side, and others' comments bear similarities to chess openings (ZZZZZZzzzzzzz.....). Game also seems to take 3 hours to play -- way too long. After a few games, it still seems too hard to get rid of the unwanted cards in your deck, and the thrill simply vaporized. I sold the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is one of the hardest games for me to rate, but I'm sticking with my 9 for now! It started as a 10, and my favourite buy from Essen 2014.

It could be downgraded to a much lower rating due to it being broken, but I'm honestly not that bothered by it. Having played GW games for 1½ decade, I'm used to house rules and gentleman agreements :)

None the less it still drags too long, due to the 'lose slowly' syndrome. And the games graphic design and user interface are amongst the worst I can remember seeing. It is as if the game is trying to be hard to read on purpose...

However, the thematic-mechanic integration is brilliant. There are so many small moments, where the deck building just shines in this context. And for that it deserves a solid 9!

RATING: 7
Ez egy nagyon ötletes játék, a hangsúly nem annyira a wargame-en van, mert nem egy keményvonalas háborús, hanem tipikus wallace játék. Szerintem veri a másik közelmúltban készült Wallace kártyásat, a Londont...
Ha egy dolgot kellene kiemelni, akkor az a hatalmas szabadság, amit a 21 féle akció kínál. Nagyon kifinomult a pakliépítés megoldása, döntően ennek kreatív használatán alapul a sikerünk vagy a kudarcunk. Igaz, hogy sokszor lutri, hogy milyen kártya van a másiknál, mégis szerintem kijön hosszú távon, hogy ki képzett jobb paklit.
Kifejezetten élveztem a sok féle lehetőséget, az egyébként meglehetősen hosszú játék javarészt elég pörgős volt, csak néha éreztem magam tanácstalannak, mert úgy "beállt" a játék. De ha valaki mer kockáztatni, akkor végig tudja pörgősen játszani. A legjobban a települések összeköttetéseinek kártyákon való leképezése tetszik. 

Az egyik hiba: kategóriájához képest elég hosszú tud lenni, bő 3 óra(de az egyik végfeltétel akár ennek töredéke alatt is bekövetkezhet). A másik hiba, hogy az angol fél erősebbnek tűnik. 

A játék egyébként nyelvfüggetlen, és viszonylag egyszerűen elsajátítható.
---
Kifelejtettem, hogy az ostrom nagyon jól ki van találva, az az egyik legérdekesebb a játékban. A másik amit ki kell még emelnem, az a kártyák tartalékba tétele (hogy ha kell, kéznél legyen), ez hatalmas ötlet, nagyon jó taktikai lehetőségeket kínál!
---
Kipróbáltam a javított kiadást is: a szerző a rajtaütés megkönnyítésével próbálja kiegyenlíteni a francia hátrányt (állítólag az első kiadáshoz találtak az angoloknak nyerő stratégiát). Nos szerintem túl jól sikerült a kiegyenlítés, a franciák rajtaütései túl erősnek tűnnek... Másodjára viszont nem tűnt annyira hosszúnak, egész pörgős volt...
---
Helyesbítés: hibának róttam fel korábban a játék hosszát, de mint kiderült, hibásan játszottuk, nálunk elfogyott MINDEN jelölője egyik félnek, nem csak a város VAGY a falu, hanem mindkettő:)

RATING: 9
Temptative rating with only one played game.  But I enjoyed it soooo much, even though it was difficult for me to remember the details.  Not difficult, really, but with several options you have to handle properly.
Really nice art (the cover is just EXCELLENT), and I really like the components of the Deluxe edition.  It's true, pieces are small, but I think they can be used with no problem
The game itself is really interesting, using a kind of Dominion mechanism.  I have not played Dominion more than a couple of times, and I hated it.  But this one, as with London also from Wallace, I find extremely nice.
The game will propably get significantly easier as you play and get a knowledge of the geography (and of course, teh actions).
All in all, and having no idea of what the Halifax Hammer is (and really don't care about it), a magnificent game.

RATING: 10
Excellent! Uncle Martin did it again. The deck building element provides countless strategic possibilities which are also intuitive and in accordance with the theme. This is one of the best wargames I've played, along with Hannibal and Washington's war.
The board lacks some needed visual aids (e.g. about what each location would yield) and it also confuses a bit about if some areas are connected or not, but it looks beautiful and it works fine. 
A great deal of strategies to try and explore. What an original use of the deck building mechanic in a wargame. This is the first and only of its kind!

Congratulations Mr. Wallace. you did it again.

edit: second play. my opinion has shifted dramatically to: excellent! (again :D ) each choice in this game is skillfully and subtly intertwined with every other thing. There won't be another one like it. It's not a trend.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Wow, what a wonderful, enjoyable, well-designed game!
It is both an economic and military game, and it's the first game that is built around the deck-building mechanic but where this mechanic is only a mean and not an end!
The game seems to have multiple paths to victory for both sides, with different possible ways to customize the deck and pursuit different strategies. It's everything but a multiplayer solitaire: there's enough interaction to force the players to pay attention and react to the opponent's moves.
There is a whole plethora of actions available, but after the first half game, everything clicks and becomes quite clear (in mechanical terms, not strategy...) and flows quickly and smoothly.
Does this sound as the game is a well mixed cocktail of mechanic? No, it also exhudes theme, both due to the components and graphics, and the gameplay.
I don't know if my initial rating (9) can hold many repeated plays, but I can't remember the last time I was so excited by a game and was not disappointed after the first play.
Hopefully Martin Wallace will use this system as a template for other games. I will buy every one of them!

Edit october 10th, 2011: after several plays, the game showed some harsh problems of balancing, with one particular strategy leading to high percentage British victory outcomes. This needs some kind of fix, because the game is great and it's a shame. Rating downgraded to 7.5 until mr. Wallace speaks about the possible fix.

Edit december 30th, 2011: official rules changes just posted on BGG by Martin Wallace. These should fix the infamous Halifax Hammer problem. Rating raised again to a 9.

Edit january 15, 2012: it seems the fix couldn't actually prevent Halifax Hammer strategies. Sadly downgraded again to 7.5...

RATING: 8

I've found this game to work much better with euro gamers as a gateway to wargaming than with wargamers as a gateway to euros. Either way, I'm still enjoying the game and I expect that I will for some time to come.

RATING: 7.5
First Play: I read Martin Wallace's foot notes at the back of the rules with interest when he said he based the card drafting on Dominion. This wasn't a good start I really got nothing out of Dominion, but this looked different. 

The rules took some sorting and explaining but the game started well amd we expanded gradually. And at this point the game hit the skids. I don't think we were bold enough. There were very few sieges, some raids and ambushes but mainly we just expanded until the towns ran out.

Overall I have to say I felt frustrated that the cards in hand just didn't allow me to do what I wanted to. At times they didn't let me do anything at all. I appreciate that you need to develop and prune an efficient draw deck and use the reserves cleverly. This was a huge learning curve game and I need to play again to test out some more theories because this first game dragged on too long and needed incisive action that neither of us executed.

Next F2F is a different affair. Yes there is still frustration at not being able to do what is wanted but its more manageable. Turns are quicker and the ability to prune the dead wood cards churns the more useful cards quicker.

Rating increased and still more room to uplift further with more plays.

RATING: 8.4
Picked up a copy of the pre-order limited edition with the wooden pieces and money.

Looks a simple game with a few cards to collect to build a deck, but there's far more to it than you would think. Casually picking up some cards "to see what they do" does not work. You are forced to plan do to the lag of getting new cards back in to your hand and you need to react to moves made by your opponent to ensure you are not completely left behind in any parcicular aspect. 

Overall, there's quite a lot of actions you can take and the wrong early choices certainly scupper your game. There's military strategies (both sieges and guerilla warfare) and expansion strategies (both settling and upgrading settlements) which help mix things up. Add income generation and hand management aspects and you have a game with a lot of replayability.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A no-brainer of a purchase for me, even as a two-player game that I know I won't get to play as much as I'll want (must check out the on-line version). Martin is my favorite game designer and I tend to camp on anything with his name on it. I do sometimes wish he had more development help though. But what enormous creativity!

Deck building, hand management, map board action, a cool theme (sorry), are just a few of the elements that excited me about this release. Pity, again, about the rules though, but I'm pretty used to this by now. It'll get straightened out and may only matter to folks who are planning on taking this to WBC - I'm really not all that competitive as long as I'm enjoying myself.

Rating is based on research and a desk-check. I'm looking forward to playing...
++++++
Finally got to play! Played two games, once on each side. Mostly Civ style so far - expanding, developing and fortifying. Second game saw more raiding and a couple of key sieges - Quebec(!) which failed, and Louisbourg which succeeded. The first game was very close with the French out-pointing the British by a couple (end game triggered by cube depletion) - in the second game the British did better by raiding more effectively than the French and by conquering Louisbourg (end game was triggered by the British managing 12 points via raids). So far, we're not seeing the dreaded imbalance, but neither of us tried the more aggressive approach let alone anything resembling a hammer. Ego not helped by losing on both sides...
++++++
Second session - this time I won both games, once on each side. In the first game I outpointed the British through development and by thwarting his attempts to raid by strategic fortressing and having the blockers at the right time. He did take Halifax and tried for Louisbourg but I was able to hold him off by aggressively ambushing his reserve so he was never able to build up the necessary mass of military for a sustained siege. The Hammer isn't as easy as it sounds - at least at our stage (but we've accepted that it is the British side's best option). During the second game I tried hard as the Brit to build up a sizable military reserve to throw at Louisbourg and Quebec. It took two attempts to finally take a fortified Louisbourg and another two to succeed at Quebec but I got it done! He was racing to exhaust his disks and raid my cubes and if I had not managed the sieges he would have won on points handily. Still I consider my win to be as much luck as good management - I was at the end of my rope militarily and if he had drawn anything on what turned out to be his final turn, I would have been sunk. At our stage at this game we are still enjoying it and not yet seeing a bad balance problem (we are using the Wallace approved rules edit). 

We may get to the stage where balance issues take some steam out of what so far we are completely enjoying, but we will cross that bridge when we get to it. Still, one of the most interesting war-game developments in some years.

ED NOTE: I'm getting a bit tired of the ranting on the Geek about the "broken" nature of this game. Some war games are tilted in favour of one of the two sides if they wish to remain thematic to the period being represented. The other entry from this period, GMT's Wilderness War, is somewhat more balanced but still favours the British. This one may be more tilted toward the British than intended but unless you are tournament level obsessive, there is no reason why you can't still enjoy this truly innovative design concept Martin has developed.
++++++
The game is not finding its way to the table much anymore mostly as my opportunities for two player games is very limited and "Mr. Madison's War" has my current attention. I've backed off my rating a bit as a result, and unsubscribed from it as I tired of the same old rants there...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
The thing I often find so frustrating about Martin Wallace designs is that he often just seems unwilling to do the work to translate his ideas into good games. AFAoS is probably the most egregious example of a game of his that is a good idea where the real work simply was not done. Gamers in general give designers way too much credit for having good ideas and not enough blame when those good ideas are not really executed on.
---
The rules changes don't seem to have fixed anything. Halifax Hammer is still the way to go. The game is just stereotyped once you figure it out, as almost all Martin Walace games are - his stuff is just too complex compared to the amount of testing that is apparently done. I'm not sure why I thought he would break out of his by now long-established track record of clever, under-developed games, but he hasn't. Sold my copy.
---
Hmm. So the rules have changed, and they've changed a *lot*. The no-location-reserve rule plus increasing raid range to two ... it's pretty huge. Seems like fixing a kink with a giant sledgehammer. I think it'll work, since reserving location cards was a non-trivial part of the original problem, but wow, as a software guy I always prefer the smallest fix possible. This is not a small fix.

On balance, I think I like the idea of AFAoS more than I like the actual game. It's the typical uneven Wallace fare. More interesting than most of his stuff just for being more imaginative, faster-playing, and tighter, but I really don't know if there is actually anything there.

Been playing this on Yucata, which I oddly think is more fun than FtF. I don't generally log internet plays, maybe I should start doing it for games like this.
---
Played once, enjoyed. Not an obviously great game, but a fun deck-builder with a nice historical veneer. Like most Treefrog games, it doesn't feel fully baked - as empires expand, especially the French to the west, it gets a little attenuated and loses momentum because the decks get too hard to wield because of the specific combos required to accomplish anything. Also, the micro-turns feel a little slow as once decks get built up a little bit it takes a bunch of time to do even simple things. Some other nagging persnickety details also. Still, the sum does add up to something interesting, and I hope it has some staying power.

I've been following the discussion of the Halifax Hammer strategy and it's relative broken-ness or not with some interest. I don't know, as I haven't played enough, but my instincts are worried about it. It's a first-principles argument that makes me nervous: basically, all deck-builders are won with tight, small, focused decks if at all possible - which is why the Chapel, and any other early-card-trashing cards are so hugely powerful in Dominion, or the Trainer in Thunderstone (and also why CCGs all have minimum deck sizes of 60 or so).  Unless a game has a systemic drag or something forcing you to acquire cards, small decks always win. Not only does A Few Acrees of Snow have all the normal biases against large decks, it's actually worse than that - as you add more locations, not only do you bloat your deck and make combos harder to draw, you also actually obsolete existing cards in your deck (because they can no longer be used to expand), thus making your deck worse in multiple ways. So if you could win with a very thin deck, you would obviously want to do that. The question is, can the French, through Raids primarily, force enough bloat into the English deck to make a game of it. My real fear is that while it may develop such that the Hammer is the best British strategy, and while it won't always win and the game may develop in interesting other directions after the initial push, it will dominate play enough to stereotype the game and strip away the illusion of choice. This is certainly a very common failing in Wallace games, something that adds to my uneasiness.

RATING: 6
Based on 1 INCOMPLETE play:

Rating is very tentative as we didn't even complete our game. But let me say first, I'm not sure how people complete this in an hour! We were on our second hour and neither of us was near winning when we called it quits. 

I don't THINK we were missing any rules - I think we just kept stalling each other by initiating attacks too early. And I suppose that's my main complaint with the game - the fact that you can even DO that. I guess I prefer games with more of a forced endgame?

Anyway, this game has some good stuff going for it, but it has a pretty high barrier of entry. In general, I like the game conceptually and in practice a lot of what I like works well - the board and combat certainly make it a more interactive deckbuilder. That said, since we mostly weren't pulling from common stacks of cards, on turns when my opponent wasn't making movement on the map I didn't really pay attention to the cards he was drafting. It certainly pays to do so, though.

I like the reserve system a lot, and I like the ability to pay to discard - with only two actions a turn and a solid cash economy, it can be a tense choice and it's a refreshing way to increase your odds of drawing good cards. Both solid additions to the genre.

Now, that barrier of entry I was talking about? The game isn't super complex, but familiarity with the game is kind of a must. Often the beauty of deckbuilders is you know right away what the use of each card you can draft is, so you have a good idea of what actions to expect in your deck. Here, there's just a lot more memorization - you might know New Haven is coming up in your deck, but what actions does it give you, again? If I settle in Albany right now, how does it affect my deck?

There's just something about the design that feels like it lacks elegance. Everything feels just a tad more complex than it needs to be. I'm interested to play a full game again now that I understand things a bit better, but as-is I'd be hard pressed to recommend this over Trains.

RATING: 7
A neat deck-building game with a French and Indian War theme. This is actually the first Martin Wallace game that I've ever played. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A huge improvement on Dominion in that there is a very solid and interesting theme here. True to history, the sides are quite asymmetric, the make up of their decks being quite different. I was impressed by the gameplay which gives players lots of options. The mechanics are very clever, particularly the use of settlement cards in settling, expanding, fortifying and laying siege actions.

I was not impressed by the wooden town pieces in the deluxe version. They are miniature versions of Settlers pieces. Full-sized ones would have been better. Plastic models (eg. from Kings & Things) would have looked better still. Also the map section of the board could have been enlarged to make better use of the board space.

Notes after one play as the French (which I lost 57-46):

Money was particularly hard to come by (compared to the British). Getting a second Pelt Trader proved useful though I think my income was still less than half that of my opponent who raked in money from trade. Income from piracy was pitiful. 

Fortifying towns as soon as possible (particularly around Nova Scotia) was also very important. 

Enlisting the two Priests was worthwhile as it reduced the number of British raids and allowed me to eventually ambush several expensive units. 

All sieges ended in defeat for the attacker (3 British, 1 French) and the only way to successfully initiate a siege against a well fortified town (starting at defence of 4) would be to play Artillery Support (cost 3) and Military Leader (as a free action) to reduce the siege points to 0. 

In hindsight, I should have picked up a green Settlers card (cost 7) required for settling and developing actions as the only other French card with settlers is the Quebec card whereas the British already have several.

Second game as the British (which I lost 53-36). My single minded strategy of trying to out-build the French failed. I missed the opportunity to settle some of the decent point scoring settlements inland.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Weird mashup of deckbuilding/wargame/abstract set in Last of the Mohicanville.  Like chess, only the way you moved your pieces by placing cards representing both the pieces and the places they can move into a deck that you control. When your strategy changes, you want to get out the pieces and locations that aren't useful to your strategy, otherwise you won't be able to move the pieces you want to very often, but if you do, you leave yourself vulnerable in the places you got rid of.  Delicious in its ability to allow you to screw yourself.

Update: the game is quite broken, sadly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
:sugar: :( :D There is a dominant strategy. It is real, and it is indeed dominant. It's a design flaw. The game does not work as designed. You will need to find a set of house rules to dodge the problem or intentionally remain ignorant of the degenerate strategy for the game to work.

Apart from that Mrs. Lincoln, how's the play?

It's really good. It's light without being fluffy. There's intriguing strategies to pursue, different theaters to contest, and the two sides are gratifyingly asymmetrical -- the Brits [i]must[/i] attack and keep the French in reactive mode, using their advantageous financial position to build up their military might, ensuring that the French don't have a free hand to develop at will. (Given that free hand, a French victory is inevitable as the tide.)

It's a deckbuilder, but it's a deckbuilder that [i]uses[/i] the mechanic in a thematically coherent matter -- your hand of cards and stock of money limits you in a way that kind of makes sense in a game of development and combat in a trackless wilderness an ocean away from reinforcements and money.

As a French and Indian War sim, Wilderness War crushes this one like a grape. But that's because of a greater degree of detail that is to be expected in a three-hour wargame vs. a ninety-minute Euro. As games -- well, I have a very soft spot for Wilderness War, so I may not be the best judge. But AFAoS holds up. It's quick and appealing. It's not as infinitely variable as Dominion, but it doesn't end up being as samey as you might think with the same cards in the supply every time. If it weren't for that whole dominant-strategy problem, I would recommend it without reservation. With it, I can't do so in good conscience. But there's a good reason why so many people were so invested in trying to save this patient.

Play it!
Fist time I rated it I give it a 7.5, but the rating is increasing with every play.
In the end I played 100+ games of this one before call it quit. My game of the year for 2012

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.75
Wallace's historical game is typical of the 2-player genre - a constant push and pull between the opposing sides, each trying to push far enough to get a win, or in this case until time runs out.

Both the British and the French are trying to settle more towns while at times looking to raid or ambush the opponent, or even siege a town.  To do this you need the right combination of cards.  These are brought into your hand and new cards are purchased in a manner that will be familiar to Dominion players.

Generally I think the game is interesting, but it definitely rewards frequent play, so experienced players will easily dominate.  Also when you get behind, it can be very difficult to catch up, particularly if your deck is full of cards you can no longer use (hint: get the Governor), or you're challenged trying to get money.  I don't mind playing online, but I don't think I need to own this.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Deeper strategy than most other 'wargames', strategy being defined as "What am I doing over the next 6-8 turns in order to win?".  Unlike most other deckbuilding games (ie, Dominion), which are purely questions of 'How fast can I churn my deck?", this one will bite you in the butt if you're not adjusting what you're doing to what your opponent is doing.

As for the 'Halifax Hammer'- its not a problem. Don't let Dominion players convince you otherwise. And if you DO think its a problem, then just match play the game.

Easy Peasey. Definitely one of the more interesting titles I've seen from Martin Wallace in ages, and I'm not that much of a fan of his games.

Darilian

RATING: 8
An excellent 2 player game.  It is fairly quick to play when you get used to it. It is asymmetrical, there  is plenty of player interaction and there are lots of different strategies to adopt and ways to counter your opponent's strategies. 

This is also another Treefrog game with a great theme. Since playing this game I have read a little about the American theatre of the Seven Years War and I am impressed about how this game reflects many of the major traits of this conflict. This is achieved both through its mechanisms and the design of the game board and cards.  I was pleased when I read about how Louisbourg was a fortified port and then took a look at the board to find that this was represented by a fortification symbol next to the settlement.

Great fun and highly recommended if you like thought provoking games dripping in theme.

I get the feeling it could have been playtested a bit more though.

RATING: 6
Love the design and mechanics. Too bad it's somewhat broken, even with the fixes. Against an expert British opponent the French are probably doomed. If they don't punch you in the face using the Halifax Hammer, they can punch you in the balls by attacking Trois Riviere from ought-not-to-be-connected-directly-to Louisburg and cutting most of your locations off in one fell swoop. To top it all off, most of the fixes in the 2nd edition actually make things [i]worse[/i] for the French.

The British simply have too many advantages in this game. Starting money, income, army size, available settlers and location connections all favor the British. Conversely, it seems there's a single best strategy for the French to counter the vaunted Halifax Hammer. That means at the end of the day, even if the HH is beatable, there appears to be a single best strategy for both players.

I don't want to sound too negative, though. I like the game, and defeating the Halifax Hammer is certainly possible in the hands of less skilled opponents who only have a vague idea how to implement it. The real fun will come for you when you and your friends are just learning the game, and you'll get a bunch of fun plays in where you're trying all sorts of wacky stuff to figure out what works and what doesn't.

Just don't play this at tournaments.

RATING: 9.6
October 2016 -9.6]
So good. Now my favorite two-player game. Great emotion.

[March 2015 - 9.0]
Continues to be a solid game and moves up the charts. Going to play with a score track the next time.

[October 2014 - 8.7]
I've only played two games, but boy do I like it. For starters, its card management is a bit different than most deck-builders in that you have to hang onto the cards you don't use, and then can only draw back up to the limit. This slows down the game, but adds great strategy and inner debate -- "do I hang onto this card for when I dearly need it, or do I blow it now for the coins ... how soon will I see it again?" The game gives you the sense of slowly building up your forces, part of the early American wars theme. I also love how one card can be so valuable at one point and so worthless at another. It really brings some emotions -- "argh, I hate this, but dammit I love it." It's hard to explain. Every other part of the game -- from city developments to sieges -- brings on that same sense of struggle and having to build up. It's also a game of potential comebacks -- I've been on the brink of defeat and have come back to win. I have only played the British in my first two plays. It's not overly long, but you have to be ready to settle in and play this game.

RATING: 5
Players are either the British or French, rapidly expanding north west North America, trying to defeat the opposition.  Large scale game covering about a hundred years, culminating in the French and Indian War.  This is a bit of an odd one, basically a deck building game where the cards are used to perform actions that can broadly be categorized as "Settling", "Fighting", "Money and Management", and "Extras".  Players on their turn take two actions from the dozen or so available, and expansion rapidly occurs as settlements and towns creep across the board.  Players will eventually bump into each other, which kicks off the raids, sieges, ambushes and so on.  While this is going on, player decks are increasing and thus diluting, so players are forced into tricky juggling tricks such as using the reserve (a holding space for cards), managing money (cards and actions can cost you) and such.  Clean game, much easier to get into than the usual over-engineered Wallace games that leave you with the feeling it wasn't worth the effort.  Having played almost every Wallace game since Stockers, this definitely feels up there with his best, such as Steam.  Very good, pity it's broken.

RATING: 8
Last night we played our first game and it was not without its bumps.  Altogether we spent 4 hours learning the rules and playing our first game.  My husband took the French and I was the British.  He started the game in good spirits and spoke only French on his turn.  Unfortunately our game kept getting interrupted.  Our daughter was being extremely annoying and needed a good hour's worth of extra attention.  Many times we had questions regarding gameplay that required consulting the rules and found no clear answer.  For instance, if your location is involved in a seige, can you still launch a raid from there?  If you have a location card in your deck, but no longer have a settlement or town at that location, can you still use it to launch a seige?

At this point, we were close to two hours into our 'game' and had each purchased maybe 3 empire cards and founded 1-2 settlements.  The game began to grate on my husband's nerves as he realized it had a very unforgiving learning curve.  He felt he had made some early mistakes in his card selection and couldn't stop dwelling on it.  We got through the next hour and I guess at this point we were both incredibly exhausted or maybe we finally hit on how to play the game because suddenly it was very fun again, just like at the beginning on the game.  Ultimately, the game ended with my husband placing all his settlements on the board.  The French were victorious, the final score being 58-50.

What we learned:

- Maybe we don't understand 'war games'; neither of us were great at seiges, and rarely was a raid successful. 

- You should really take a look at the location card before you choose to found a settlement.  Some cards are just going to be dead in your deck; they give you little to no options on using them later.  

- Purchasing empire and neutral cards need to be careful decisions.  Don't rush a card into your deck before you are ready for it (fur trader, I'm looking at you!)

- The reserve is crucial to launching a successful seige.  You can't count on drawing the attack cards you need to continue your battle so be prepared! 

My thoughts on the game:

It was hard, and frustrating, and damnit, I loved every second of it.  I was up all night in bed, laying awake, going over the different cards in my head.  There have only been a few games in my life that have captivated my mind as much as this one.  I am desperate to play it again and get better.

RATING: 10
One word:  Wow!  Marrying deckbuilding to a push-pull wargame was fantastic design by Martin Wallace.  Impressive too in that the British and French decks are very different, but the game is very balanced in spite of this asymetry.  Very strong and well applied theme, despite being a card game - not a war game!

The Halifax Hammer is not a really a problem and there are fixes and simple house rules to restrict a player from trashing  most of his cards.  After this, there's no guarantee a water invasion will succeed by game end and although this was the historical path taken by the British during the war, there are many other paths to victory: seizing Quebec by land (instead of water) or else on points by blocking the French settlement.  The French have a vey good chance of winning too, by establishing forts in the Ohio River Valley and wearing the British down.

Highly recommended as a great advancement on the deckbuilding genre.

RATING: 6

[i]
UPDATE from MW:

1. Remove the French bateaux card from the initial deck and place it with the available cards.

2. Reserve - you cannot place location cards in your reserve.

3. Raids - the raid distance is now two connections plus one connection for each additional card played (so playing two Native American cards would allow you to raid a location up to three connections away).

4. Home Support - you can only take cards from your draw deck, so if you had fewer than three cards available you would only be able to draw these. Consequently, the only time you shuffle your discard pile is when you need to refill your hand at the end of the turn and your draw deck is exhausted.
[/i]

RATING: 2
The idea of marrying deck building to a tactical board game is great. The result, however, is the usual Wallace hodgepodge of ideas (of late) where some of the gears engage and others spin pointlessly.

The area around Louisbourg becomes a hot spot since Louisbourg is a key gateway to Quebec. It's such a hotspot that it is ultimately a distraction from any other plans you might have for the game; reduced to settling and developing territories when you're not swatting military flies. And maybe that's accurate historically, but it's a huge distraction here.

The whole native Americans and raiding piece will only ever make sense if both players are pursuing a relatively peaceful settlement strategy with no emphasis around Louisbourg.

So, still trying to figure out if it's ultimately a good game or not. Right now it's interesting, with promise, but I've been burned by too many Wallace promises.

[Update]
Played a proposed (possibly official) fix and it makes the game a good bit better by making raids more viable. So you don't just have to match each other in how you play; there are some options now.

[Update]
Life's too short for Wallace's half-baked ideas. Done.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
subscription 2010/due March 2011/Martin Wallace, signed & numbered

This is an area control war game with a very unique and smart deck-building mechanic. Martin Wallace has again incorporated his historical interests into a thematically rich encounter, this time between the French and the British in North America.   A very asymmetric design lays the foundation for multiple strategies and results in re-playability.  

All the deck building games I have played (which is almost all of them) rely on thinning the deck for faster, slicker wins. This one is no different, however, as the game changes, the needed composition of the deck does too, perhaps at some point even reverting, and those cards which might have earlier become useless and discarded can be re-entered into the hand.  I find that the deck in this game needs to be more flexible, and that I need to be ready to adapt quickly to changing game conditions. 

The main objection I have to this game is that it takes so long to play--one of the strong points for me of deck building games is that they most often are fast and furious.  Even if  I play this enough (and I am sure I will) to reduce the game play to an hour as the box suggests,that is still longer than I like to spend on a 2-player, deck-building game.  It is a disadvantage, but not a big one.  

Martin Wallace has again designed an original game, one that deviates from his other game designs.  This fresh game is another added to the long list that underscores his credibility as an important, premier game designer.

RATING: 9
Limited edition (#231)

Very clever, thematic and tense game.

Clever because of the deck-building: it is smoothly integrated into the game, it brings a lot of choice and combinations, it simulates the required delay between the gain of a card and its use. The sieges are very well simulated : pretty abstract but not too much. Brillant !

Thematic because of the asymmetry between :
- french and english decks
- costal (ships, settlers and money) and inland (bateau, fur) locations

Tense because the game makes you involved all the time. It is a "take that" game, makes of sudden raids, piracy, sieges, ambushes to weaken your opponent.

This game is a matter of timing and choices : you must pay attention to your deck's efficiency not to reach your goals but also to adapt to your opponent dirty tricks.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very nice blend of asymmetric deckbuilder, euro and light wargame with a fitting theme (the battle between France and Great Britain for the control of upper North America).

The game can end by conquest, raiding, settling or expanding your colonies. The cards dictate which actions you can take. They are either locations on the board (which are acquired by settling) or special cards bought from the empire deck, like natives, fortifications, infantry or artillery.
Managing the deck and knowing which cards are coming and which your opponent holds in his hand is vital to success. Settling too much will fill your deck with useless godforsaken locations that will slow you down; hiring too many natives may make you adept to raiding and ambushing, but sooner or later they'll fill your hand and you'll wish you had never hired them. The same can be true for your military: it's awesome for sieging, but at the end of a siege everything will be dumped into your discard pile and will return in your deck to haunt you. The governor is a card you'll learn to love: he will bring the needed order and discipline to your deck, allowing you to remove unwanted or unneeded cards and return as slim and fit as ever.

The game's balance has been questioned and it has been shown that the British hold a decisive advantage, but it's hard to execute the perfect strategy, and I think the game is still very interesting.

RATING: 8
I really enjoy playing this game. The way that a deck building mechanism, that is tightly integrated with the theme of the game, has been woven into a light wargame. Except it's not really a war game, it's about supply and logistics. It's a really nice package, cosmetically as well. It looks lovely on the table.

I was aware of the issues with the HH but avoiding reading about it. I think that we did eventually work out how to roughly implement it after a number of games but probably not as efficiently as other, more proficient players. Still, the vague knowledge of this did funnel the British down this route and large areas of the board were rarely visited.

Then we started to play the official variants and this opened up the game entirely. More variety, different goals, different tactics.

I can't see myself tiring of this one.

RATING: 10
I really love that game. IMO it perfectly combines deck building with area control/war game. "Trains" tries something similar but IMO never reaches the depth of AFAOS.
 
Since you have to keep unused cards on your hand for the next turn (unless you're willing to spend 1 of your actions) there are a lot of tough decisions to make.

Good timing is a huge factor and makes it even a little more interesting to me than Dominion.

I know there is an ultimate strategy to blow away the French. But I really don't care about it. Every game I played so far was very different from the previous ones. I've won games with very different strategies and I also lost with different strategies (with both the French & the British).

This game has lots of replayability. Unfortunately it is only a 2 player game. But that really is the only downer to me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
Strategy feels very limited by the way combat is performed. Because of the way sieges work, a player has to mount a defense as soon as the other player makes an attack. Then because of how players' assets become tied up in that siege, simultaneous offensives seem nearly impossible (save a simple base race, of course, if you don't defend any battles). Accruing lesser defenses will allow those assets to remain bound in that combat, but both players will have a similar amount of effort put into both attack and defense, making that attrition pointlessly luck-based.
Raiding is a frustrating experience since in order to prevent a raid, a player has to be lucky enough to have a particular card in hand when the opponent decides to raid.
More than anything else, it is regrettable that the Halifax Hammer so totally invalidates any other strategy and that it is not contra to the sensibilities of other deck building games.

First Impressions: 1

RATING: 5
After a trip to the coast where we played this pretty much non-stop for two days, I feel qualified to call FAoS very very good. Unlike all those other deckbuilding games out there, this is filled with interaction, conflict, and excitement. Fast-moving with tense decisions. Like a Netrunner set in the 18th Century. 

If it weren't already apparent by now, Martin Wallace is a game designer maestro. 

Update: More plays only strengthen my appraisal. Few Acres is a brilliant design and a solid classic. Raised my rating.

Update: After the sad discovery of the unbeatable British siege strategy, I have no choice but to lower my rating. Until this matter can be repaired, the game no longer functions as designed.

Update: The anticipated rules fix by Martin Wallace seems more a band-aid than a fix. As he himself has stated, any game of this sort without game-to-game base rule changes has an inherent dominant strategy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Deck building driven by a board game - there are no piles of cards in a poole from which to buy. Rather you add cards by establishing settlements or conquering your opponents settlements (adding a "location" card for the settlement) or use an action to draft, and pay for, cards that you need from a single poole pile of cards from which you can select whatever card your strategy requires. Cards allow you to expand your domaine by adding settlements (initially villages that can be upgraded to towns for double points at game end), or you can attack your opponent by sieging or raiding his settlements or ambushing him to make him remove cards from his deck. Most cards serve multiple purposes as defined by easy to identify and understand icons. Component quality is outstanding (I had the pre-order copy of the game with the wooden bits). I hope that this is the beginning of a whole series of this type of game.

Unfortunately a balance issue appears to have broken the game - bugger.

RATING: 8
At first there seemed to be soooo many possible actions that the AP in this game would be killer.

Sometimes it is....and yet, overall it gives me that same vibe as playing Twilight Struggle towards the mid-endgame. It's much tighter quarters and the rules are easier to understand and digest. It's a brilliant game with a unique theme that works! (Not too many games about Canada...?)

Many people complain that there is a 'broken' strategy, and I URGE you to ignore reading anything about that! Enjoy this game, it's a really great design that deserves to be explored! (Twilight Struggle + Dominion = A Few Acres of Snow...without a doubt!)

RATING: 4

RATING: 6
This is a 2-player game that seems a little like a war game (because you can play a military strategy to raid/ambush/besiege your opponent).  However, the game can end if a player develops all their villages into towns, for example, and then it goes by how many points each player has on the board (and in captured towns/villages).

The cards for each side are very different -- the British get a lot of money from their original cities, whereas the French get a lot from trader beaver pelts and even playing Piracy on the British.  Since the French have so few cards with settlers (only Quebec, not even any Empire cards in their own deck), it is imperative that they pay 7 to recruit at least one neutral (green) Settlers card, or even both of them.

Rules note: you can't put a location card in your Reserve, only Empire cards (whether your own colour or neutral green ones).

RATING: 8
(11/14/12) Initial play. This is a fascinating coupling of a deck-building mechanism with a spatial aspect, to convincingly convey the "fog of war". Martin Wallace has said that he always starts with a theme, and develops game mechanisms around the theme. There is hardly a fleeting thought that any of his designs are simply game mechanisms with pasted on themes, which tends to be the case with most Euro designs. Wallace succeeds, most impressively, on a number of levels with [i]A Few Acres of Snow[/i].

The rules and game mechanisms are quite simple, although the decision tree is involved. Due to the sheer number of choices, it is possible to be overwhelmed at first, but with a few turns play becomes straightforward and quick. What continues to linger after play is how rich and immersive the game play was, and that the design was able to convey that feeling with very few rules and components. The deck-building aspect faithfully recreates the uncertainties of war, especially conflict in the frontiers of northern New England / Canada, during the French-Indian War. A player could draft additional troops or specific cards, but with the deck-builder, it could be some time before he could make use of such a card. The same player could colonize a strategic location on the board and receive a location card, but again, it could be some time before he could see the card and thus activate a neighboring town for expansion. Players could elect to draft neutral cards depicting Indian forces, for unpredictable and chaotic raids along the frontier, for equally devastating effect.

Everything in this game simply works, and works well. Both of us enjoyed the British/French conflict narrative unfold before our very eyes. Starting with the major cities, both sides would slowly expand into the frontier, until they were literally a town away from making violent contact. Sieges and raids were modeled abstractly, but effectively conveyed the feel. It quickly became apparent that management was important, finding the equivalent "Chapel", or "Council" card, to thin the deck, or to bring more cards into play. Cycling through the deck was a furious and frantic affair on both sides, searching for key cards to settle, lay siege, reinforce, raiding, fortifying, and so forth. Would I be able to get a "fortification" card in time and build up defenses, before my opponent launched a siege against a key city? Did my opponent have a card or two, to counter my raid? Was it worth more to raid his shipping lanes and keep him poor, or was it worth more to use the same card to settle a key location on the board? Delicious, dicey decisions abound on both sides, where every action was fraught with uncertainty and tension.

While I am keenly aware of the "Halifax Hammer", which is supposed to break this game, we were not experienced enough to recognize it. It may become apparent with a number of plays, but I am prepared to switch over to the new rules when the time comes. The game is as enjoyable as my first plays of London, which is another brilliant Wallace design focusing on card/hand management. It is easy to see why this game has received so much attention, with plenty of passionate discourse on the various forums; it has all the hallmarks of a "great" game and/or great game design, despite the one mentioned flaw. I'm looking forward to playing it again. My initial rating is an '8'. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Martin Wallace does Dominion, and like most of MW's games it's not as elegant as its immediate competitors. It's an oddball wargame, where your actions are limited to the cards you draw from a deck you build. It can be heavy weather getting anywhere as to move (for example) you need both a location card and a means of transport appropriate to your destination, and getting both cards in your hand simultaneously can be a frustrating experience. One major flaw is that the French should have their own 'Settlers' card as the English can grab both neutral copies near the start and this seriously restricts the French player's options. I've pretty much given up on Martin Wallace these days, his games contain good initial ideas, but there's never enough refinement for his games to really shine. This does nothing to change my opinion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
So here's my take on the whole Halifax Hammer issue: how you feel about it and the effect it has on A Few Acres of Snow is largely dependent on you as a gamer.  If you are someone who ruthlessly pursues the best strategy by any means necessary (and from what I understand, HH is a series of moves that need to be followed very specifically in order to work, and is very difficult to figure out without researching it on the forums) and refuse to deviate from that strategy because it is "the best one", then AFAoS is probably not for you.  If, however, you are able to put aside (okay, fine, [i]ignore[/i]) the controversy and explore the game on your own terms, you will probably be as taken in by the game's charms as I was.  I'm not casting judgement on either position, just saying that the existence of the Halifax Hammer is enough to disqualify it as a game worth playing for a certain percentage of people, while for others it will be a complete non-issue.

Which is a shame, really, because this is really a great game.  The integration of the deck building and wargaming is incredibly seamless and satisfying, the theme and overall look of the game is superb, and the game is strategically and tactically interesting while not being overly weighty.  It's Martin Wallace at his best - innovative use of mechanics perfectly meshed with an interesting historical theme to create a compelling gaming experience.

RATING: 1
This game is quite pleasant, the mechanic is good. I tend to dislike deck building games when it's actually just for the sake of deck building, but this one provides a good theme and a way to actually use your deck building as a part of a strategy to beat the opponent.

I was quite pleased at this game on the first couple of hours playing, until I found out how broken the game is. I felt it when playing the French, and even more when playing the English. Once discovered and exploited, this flaw is fatal. No point in playing this anymore.

EDIT:
Upon publishing (supposedly) fixed rules Wallace wrote:

MEGA LOL!!! :D

Quote of the century...

--

I'm amazed when people stress what an original and fresh design it is.  This is - really - pretty standard design in deck-building genre...

It would've been the same rating as Dominion as it is, in fact, Dominion with the map added and set in the historical framework.

BUT:

Many different approaches and strategies seemed to exist in the game.  That is until always successful British strategy was found. In short - the games is BROKEN (-> RATING DOWN ACCORDINGLY).

Even if not that broken strategy, the problem is - for me - that I have no desire to explore potential strategies and possibilities here.  I'm just no deck-building fan and in addition the game is very very abstract, only loosely connected to its historical background.

Not the game for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Well after much deliberation and months of watching the HH threads I finally decided to pick it up and play it for myself. Also, while it seemed that no perfect solution was found, some workable ones were definitely cropping up and that was enough for me to take the leap since I have no fear of tinkering. So I never did pay attention to what the HH was exactly in case I bought the game, and it never came into play to my knowledge in first game. 

So the first thing that struck while reading the rules was THESE ARE THE BEST TREEFROG RULES EVER! Damn, ASIE weren't too bad but these were near bulletproof so maybe the old days of the infamous treefrog rules are finally gone. In short, just damn clear and logical rules and really tight, I had no questions after reading, and none cropped up while playing either other than why were ship cards 6 bucks (and looking at threads many many people had the same question) so not a rules question but a design choice question. So with this its pretty easy to learn imo, only about 10 pages to read and most of that is all the wealth of actions you have available (which I love, options are almost always a good thing). The game is dead simple rules-wise really, and after 1/3 of a game or so, it starts to flow as you learn how and when to do these actions, and right about then you also realize some mistakes you already made too.... 

So the best thing about the game to me is the flexibility you get with the deck building, which really allows you to tailor your approach to the game big time. Its also not like London where the board is just tacked-on, here the board is front row and center to the play. It sort of fits into the twilight struggle category of wargames, as in has a great war theme and wants to be but isn't quite, and its close enough that people will debate it. You don't have any troops you can move around and attack with per se, but in a TS way you can attack neighboring spots with cards from your hand and expand out that way. I think the important thing here to note is that board positioning and approach are almost as important as timing and what cards you are adding to your hand, so it all comes together nicely imo. 

In short, this is what London should have been and it really shows how pedestrian London is in comparison. I'll have more to say I think after another game or two, I can tell you we definitely grew our decks too big too fast in our first game which is often a no-no in a DBG, and what we found was that there were many "non-turns" where there wasn't much you could do because of the cards we were drawing. Some of this I can see can't be avoided but some I am pretty sure can so more play to confirm. For me personally I have noted many times that a pure DBG bores me to tears, but when you make it just one element of a bigger game like here or MageKnight it really shines and lets the creative juices flow.

UPDATE - Just a small update as I can't seem to shake a couple of feelings here as after-thoughts (definitely could be wrong/off on these but they are nagging at me a little). 

1 - I'm thinking the game on the map is going to play out fairly similar each game. I think you are going to have some flexibility in your approach to how you build your deck (but see 2) but since you start with the same locations each game, and those link the same locations, which you then add to deck, and then there are really not that many on the board either so this is how it looks to me for now. I guess if you had more, and more "paths" to expand to the game would end up longer, also this "samey map development" could be tagged on many games too, anyway just an observation 

2 - I wish the game was a little more dynamic (though I don't think this is quite the word I'm looking for). It just seems many turns you have nothing to do, you have 2 cards to settle but missing one, you can't quite start a seige, you can't quite fortify etc, like 40% of the time you get matching cards and the rest of the time you might even discard cards as an action just to try and get other cards. Now this could be us growing our decks too fast, but I'm also thinking this is just a trait of the game you have to accept as well and part of the card draw luck here. Maybe a 6 card hand size would help... Anyway let's see how game 2 goes, as we made a few mistakes in game 1 and are now all the wiser in other areas too.  

UPDATE - Playing the 2nd game and it really struck me just how much of a strategic game this is, it is ALL planning and prioritizing in all aspects of your play. Which cards too add, when and in what order. Which cities to settle, when and in which order. Which cities to fortify, when, order, etc, etc, and etc. This permutates into everything you do which to me is fantastic. Its is also critical here to watch which cards your opponent is adding in order to be prepared to react to potential threats. Another note the asymmetry is done pretty well too. I might have to increase my rating here hammer or not. 

UPDATE 7.5 => 7 Played with a much slimmer deck and while that makes it easier to get things done, there are still a good few 'non-turns' here. Basically more play is not alleviating my 2 concerns I noted above, its a pretty neat game but it just seems to me could have used just a little more development (not pointing to HH here, but obviously that would have been found too) While you have some wiggle room in your deck building, it seems to me each player's overall approach to the game is going to be 'fairly' similar (not exact) each game (more play could prove this somewhat inaccurate) 

=========

Pure deckbuilding barely qualifies as a game to me but makes a good filler, though it mostly leaves me cold. Akin to a pure drafting game without anythng added and thankfully someone finally thought to add an actual game around the deckbuilding, and it looks to be a good one.

Definitely want to play this and do need to play after London which was only lukewarm for me

Edit - Ah well, no big surprise the game has some balance issues, at least its not to me. You start pumping out too many games in a year, means less play-testing for each and something is going to give. WAS going to likely buy this even though I don't like deck building much as the rest of the game sounded interesting, very unlikely now. Let's see how houserules play out....

RATING: 8
First impression after two games: The card system is gamey and gimmicky as hell, but fun all the same. I can't really stand Dominion anymore, but wrapping its central mechanism in a historical wargame makes for great fun, if not exactly a feeling of much historicity.

The game rides a fine line between wargame and deckbuilding eurogame, without feeling quite like either. It's not going to revolutionize wargaming - it's much too much euro and too little wargame for that - but one still gets quite a lot of historical feel as part of the card play. And seen as a deck-builder, it's just brilliant.

Anyway, I enjoyed my first couple of games immensely and can't wait to try it again. I keep going over potential strategies in my head - a good sign, definitely! :)

RATING: N/A
Second Edition Rule Changes:
1. Remove the French bateaux card from the initial deck and place it with the availble cards.

2. Reserve - you cannot place location cards in your reserve.

3. Raids - the raid distance is now two connections plus one connection for each additional card played (so playing two Native American cards would allow you to raid a location up to three connections away).

4. Home Support - you can only take cards from your draw deck, so if you had fewer than three cards available you would only be able to draw these. Consequently, the only time you shuffle your discard pile is when you need to refill your hand at the end of the turn and your draw deck is exhausted.

RATING: 7
Incredible game with a relatively straightforward, simplistic design that reveals amazing strategic depth. It also mixes the wargame, economic development, and deck-building genres into a seamless, fast-paced 2-player game that can be finished in about an hour. A perfect storm of awesomeness. I liken it to a BarbaRossa/Agricola hybrid　（BarbaRossa for the army-deckbuilding, and Agricola for the difficult choices to be made with very limited available actions). This game could be destined to be one of my all-time favorites.

EDIT: After failing to find a counter for the British Halifax Hammer strategy, my rating dropped from a 9.5 to a 7. Here's hoping Martin finds a fix for it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Four plays in, keeps getting better.

Love the gameplay. Good use of Dominion's deck-building engine in a game, in that (unlike the source) the game comprises of more than just the engine itself.

I'm begining to see some of the options. I really like the way there's always a counter to any apparently strong strategy, and that you need to determine how much effort to put into thwarting your oppoent versus pursuing your own goals.

To avoid falling behind you really can't let your hand clog up with junk, you have to keep the cards turning over and weed out the no-longer-useful from your deck.

Likely to take a fair number of plays to explore all the available options.

RATING: 8.5
After having played online many times and in person a couple times, I can say without hesitation that this is the most difficult deck builder. Since you are directly facing against someone, the confrontation is very intense and in your face. This game is not very forgiving. 
I think that it would take about 2 plays to start playing well. You really need to weigh your decisions. 

If that doesn't turn you off from this game then I highly recommend you give it a look. This game is really unlike anything you have ever played before. Every decision feels heavy and you really need to consider your options before you make a decision. The gears behind this game are very tight and decisions can have long lasting effects. 

I absolutely adore this game and am not alone.

RATING: 4
Wallace didn't succeed in fixing this. Can't rate a broken game higher than this. 
------
Too bad that I made the mistake to browse BGG forums: [thread=703495][/thread]. As usual with deckbuilders, there are annoying / degenerate deck thinning strategies that are well beyond my understanding as a non-MTG player but are necessary to be applied when winning is the only goal. Deckbuilding coupled with asymmetric sides worsens the situation and easily leads to broken games (e.g. Campaign Manager 2008 - just take a look at the best players winning ratios at yucata.de and you know what I mean). Knowing that there are superior strategies for one side that can't be countered diminishes my interest close to zero. Hopefully, Wallace issues a fix that will open up the field of possible winning strategies again. Until then, 4/10.

Deckbuilding+, i.e. there's a purpose to deckbuilding and there's a spatial component to the game which puts this ahead of other deckbuilders. I really enjoyed it on my first play. A firm 7/10 so far.

RATING: 6
This is a game I expected to love, but I could never get that much into it. I wasn't even bothered by the "broken" HH strategy (though that entire argument on BGG was beyond ridiculous), because I never attempted to play it. There were so many parts of the game that I liked, as well as many interesting choices to make, but it just didn't come together for me. Some of that, no doubt, is due to the theme (which I don't find the least bit interesting). Some of it could be due to the fact that deck-building is by no means my favorite game mechanic. AFAoS is definitely an ambitious attempt to take deck-building and build a serious game around it, and for that it deserves heaps of praise. But overall I'd say that this is a pretty good game that got slammed by a bunch of geeks' overly-inflated expectations. Pity.

RATING: 8
Combines a fabulous blend of tactics (deck-building) with strategy (board campaign).  There are multiple ways to win and you have an economic victory or a war victory. Timing is important and one needs to keep aware of what your opponent is planning for: by watching (1) what cards are acquired for which purposes and by seeing (2) which locations are gained.  The main key to understanding this game is that you have to use many hands (tactics) to take baby steps in implementing your board play (strategy) - and try not to switch strategies back and forth, it only further delays your intermediate goals.  This is another brilliant game by Martin Wallace and it is superb in play and rich in theme.  What a gem!

RATING: 6
Uh oh, appears the game is broken?  That would be quite disappointing.  Decided to play anyways and will not look at the methodology involved in the Halifax Hammer.

First play was interesting despite quite a few poor decisions in my deckbuilding efforts early.  One's deck can really get quite large and unwieldy if one is not careful!  Each side has a specific advantage which probably should be specifically pursued.  French probably need to focus on settling early and building forts if possible as well as raiding with the Native Americans.  Raiding is quite interesting and can be quite powerful although forts can really blunt the impact of raiding so the British should likely build forts to protect themselves.  Culling decisions are not always easy.  Concept of this game is fascinating - just wish there wasn't such a dominant strategy for the British that basically breaks the game from all accounts.  Maybe I'll never discover that strategy.

RATING: 8.5
Interesting deck building wargame.  This is the first game I have seen that takes the basic deck building mechanic and does something radically different than Dominion.  The addition of the board and the way in which siege combat is handled is quite groundbreaking.  Ultimately, it could probably offer some more wargame flavor (perhaps greater differentiation of units -- maybe with actual unit designations) to put it over the top and make it a favorite game but it does what it was intended to do quite well and pushes a popular genre in an innovative direction.

RATING: 9
Enjoyed it, without being blown away.

It was nice to explore many of the possible actions.  

Playing by myself was helpful, as this forced me to clarify a few points left unclear in the rules (no raid route from/to Tadoussac), etc.  Sufficient clarifications were found in the FAQ and on BGG.

There are enough strategic possibilities that I felt a little unfocused: should I expand through villages, or develop cities, or raid, or besiege?  Not knowing the point tally also did not help with this: I felt less tension than if I had known that I had to catch up, of that I was not ahead by much.

The rules are streamlined enough that they were not a big problem.

I wish that the player aids that come with the game were more precise (for example, I think that some free actions are not marked as such, or something like that).

Small details show that the game was printed a little too fast: a few (inconsequential) typos in the rulebook, the misprint on the British Fort Venango card, the fact that it's printed as "Venago" on the board…  Not a huge problem, though.

After a first, partial solo play, I don't feel a strong desire to play it again, though.  Not that I don't like the game.  I'm probably not as tempted to play again because this game is relatively static: the decks evolve slowly, pieces don't move/change much,…  I enjoy the idea of playing a more varied game than A Few Acres….  Maybe it's just a matter of mood, however.  The fact is that I feel more like playing older but more dynamic/bloody games like Commands & Color: Ancients, or Memoir'44, or Claustrophobia; or older games like Race for the Galaxy (the hands/card powers vary greatly from turn to turn).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5

RATING: 7.5
Has Martin Wallace flown too close to the sun?

I have played this once, before the furore that has surrounded the game; the emergence of a strategy for the which guarantees one side victory.  I was lax in putting up my comments but was inspired by the excellent post of Jesse Dean (http://www.boardgamegeek.com/blogpost/9073/a-few-acres-of-snow-and-the-critical-silence-on-th) to reconsider the game and my attitudes towards it.

Furthermore, my revisiting the game was done while admiring Bubba Watson's 2012 Master's triumph, who Ed Smith of The Times commented, 'We thrill to triumphs such as Watson's far more than victories founded on the calculated elimination of error.'  Could the same be said about the game?

To draw a line it is empirically evident that the game is not perfect.  It is flawed and that is the responsibility of the designer and the playtesters.  This has been proven over time (a relatively short period of time) and for the game now to suceed at any level you have to treat the flaw like the elephant in the room. 

However, in my single experience of the game I found it to be an exercise in inventiveness, ambition and daring and therefore I would be willing to play again.  Firstly, because the game is ambitious, challenging and enjoyable.  Though I played badly (I always do) I was able to switch strategies , regroup and still had a chance to snap victory from the jaws of defeat.  Secondly, while the game is unsuitable for tournament play I play the majority of my games in a less intense environment with individuals who do not read boardgamegeek or even know what it is and we would not be familiar with the strategy or get any joy from playing a single pre-defined strategy.  Therefore the solved / broken nature of the game is of less importance to me and my group of friends.  Finally, and a small factor is that this game is an important moment in game design (the fusion of the mechanisms).  Though other games will come along and do a better job is undeniable but this was the first to push the boundaries.  Not exactly on the scale of the Wright Brothers but an interesting move forward.

I am of the opinion therefore that the game is solved rather than broken and would happily play again with the proviso that the well publicised solution was not used.

RATING: 9
This game is an absolutely brilliant implementation of the card-drafting mechanic to re-imagine the very idea of a wargame. As the notes in the back of the rulebook make clear, the feeling of fiddling away one's time while one waits for the perfect hand to come along is actually a plausible simulation of the bizarre manner in which way that the French and British conducted the Seven years' War from an enormous distance away. 

If you forget about the theme, gameplay can sometimes feel like a bit of a slog, and there are a lot of quirky rules about card use to keep in one's head. But it's a work of singular genius, nonetheless.

RATING: 7
Interesting twist on deck-building.  I've only played on Yucata thus far (and it is somewhat difficult to learn there), so I'm not sure I quite have it figured out.  

The strategy for the Brits does seem straight forward enough.  But, the French likewise should be agressive from the start, it seems.

I'd like to play face to face a few times and see if it changes the feel of the game.  I think it is hard for me to 'see' the game as the map play is so driven by the location cards.

The alternate rules do shake things up a bit.  This is classic Martin Wallace in that it is a brilliant design with a fatal flaw.  I only wish that there is a way to bring some balance back with alternate rules.
