RATING: 8
June 2017 - free
Wow! First time going to a Canucks Amuck meetup and a guy walks in and says he has a trunk full of games he is giving away for free. One of which is this game, which I almost bought from someone not 2 days prior.

I generally don't enjoy pure deck-builders unless there are other aspects to the game in addition. I really love Wallace's A Study in Emerald, both editions, for example. The deck building is central here and I do feel it, so I have something of a hard time wrapping my head around the how to best play. I will likely never be a good player, but I don't mind playing this at all. Most especially because my group would all be pretty equal to me in experience. I expect it would be aggravating to play this with very experienced players.

As far as the game being broken...maybe it is...but it only becomes so once you know how to set up and then exploit the winning strategy. Until then, the play experience is still worthy.

RATING: 6
I’ve read a couple of weeks ago that this otherwise highly lauded game carries a fatal flaw in that a certain strategy makes one of the two sides impossibly hard to beat. Playing the game for the first time with that fact in mind (without being able to recollect what, exactly, that strategy is) was a funny experience. Without that information, it would have been a very enjoyable first step into a game that was obviously demanding - it is, for example, hard to create a working game plan without firm knowledge of the various location cards you can gain - but had enough depth and good ideas to reward the time spent to master it. Alas, knowing what I knew, it felt like trying to make a dead bird fly; after all, the game would ultimately not stay true to its promise of being a well-developed asymmetrical war game. Well, the asymmetrical part is true, obviously.

What’s also funny is that for almost every other designer, I would be nowhere near as fast to believe the word of mouth here on the Geek regarding perceived imbalances in a game, but with Wallace’s track record in recent years, the real surprise would have been getting an least at adequately developed game designed by him. This is all the more galling because the design is admittedly brilliant, and I can well understand the many fans of the game who try to find a fix for it. Won’t somebody stop this man from releasing too many games in too little time and make him properly polish one of his efforts for once?

RATING: 4
This is simply a brilliant game. There's absolutely nothing about it that I don't love. I rated it a 9 after one play...then learned about the Halifax Hammer. Now I can't imagine playing it again without trying to work out how HH is done, despite actively avoiding learning how it's done. It's the elephant in the room that will prevent me from ever playing this again; it sucks. I sold the game because of this flaw, and re-rated it to a 4.

Setting that aside, the rulebook is stellar, the bits are nice, the board and graphic design are deliberately understated in a way I find appealing, the artwork is great -- and, most importantly, it's fun and satisfying to play. One play barely scratches the surface, because it's packed so full with options.

Individual turns don't take too long because in general your hand keeps your options limited -- but this is a strategic deck building game, so the hand you have is the hand you built. That adds a ton of depth. The randomness introduced by the card draw adds more, and fits the theme; the balance of strategic/tactical decisions is excellent.

That this amazing package is wrapped around a game that also brilliantly models the history it's trying to model is kind of mind-boggling. Supply lines were long during this period, which is represented by new resources going into your discard pile; settling/conquering a location gives you access to that place's resources, but also makes it harder to manage your empire, which is modeled by increasing the size of your deck.

On top of that, Alysia (non-wargamer) and I (lapsed casual wargamer) made it through our first play in two hours, and I don't think we screwed up any rules -- but it wasn't a light or fluffy experience in any way: It was just right.

There's not an ounce of fat here, and the meat is delicious. Hell, even the box cover is great -- it's stunning, and one of the most intriguing I've ever seen.

RATING: 7
A Few Acres of Snow is an asymmetrical, two-player, deck building, war/civilization board game that simulates the Seven Years' War in North America.  That was a mouthful!  In it players take on the role of either the English or the French, each having their own starting colonies.  Each player has their own starting deck of cards, and in typical deck building fashion they can use these cards to get more powerful cards as the game plays on.  These cards are then used to take actions on the game board, which depicts a map of the eastern third of North America.  There are lots of actions that can be taken on a player's turn.  Some of the more common ones are: get money, develop a new settlement, increase a settlement from a village to a town, fortify a town, and lay siege to an enemy settlement.  There are a few different win conditions.  The most war-like is take Quebec if you are the English, or take either Boston, or New York if you are the French.  The more civ-building win condition is to put either all your villages, or all your towns out on the board before the other guy.

I really enjoy A Few Acres of Snow.  I like the historical aspect of the game.  I like that there are many options that can be taken on your turn.  Players can use different strategies to go after a win.  You can be as war oriented as you like, or just try to expand your influence peacefully.  If you like history, or strategy, or deck building, or good games...then I highly recommend you give this one a try.

RATING: 7
Halifax hammer be damned. A Few Acres of Snow is a light wargame with Dominion-inspired gameplay and, at that, it works very well. AFAoS allows for aggressive and defensive strategies through multiple potential victory conditions. For my part, I've found winning any sieges to be excessively difficult and a considerable investment of effort. The game seems, primarily, won on settling and raiding.

That being said, it balances quite well. It has bit of a learning curve but, once acclimated, will play fairly smoothly.

RATING: 2
Martin Wallace polarises me. Some of his games I love (Wensleydale, Automobile, London, Liberte), others I really don't care for. This is, I'm afraid, one of the latter. A deck-building CDG (sort of), you have a hand of cards which can be played as one of your two actions a turn. Replenishment also costs an action. An action may consist of more than one card played - for example, play a town card to indicate that you're moving a force to a town, play another to represent the method of travel, plus another, if required, to represent frontierspeople/settlers. 

There's been a lot of this kind of thing lately and, though it's not a bad game, it's not my kind of game. I find the two actions per turn too limiting. Sorry, Martin, but I pass on this one. (And the Discworld one too as I think Discworld is lame.)

RATING: 5
French vs the British for the conquest of Canada otherwise known as the French Indian War that culminated in the fall of Quebec in 1759. A gateway wargame with a novel deck building mechanic.  This game is a little gem.  Fast for a wargame and extremely playable and even spouse friendly yet with layers of strategy that are only apparent after several plays.  Love it!  Rated:  9

UPDATE:  After two weeks I and several others have discovered a strategy that breaks the agme.  I've quit playing it and am downgrading it unless Martin responds as promised with some kind of fix. Rated: 5 (Oct 28/2011)

RATING: 7
Decent game. I still need to play more to determine how much control I have. I don't like how you start the game with a large portion of your victory points already in hand. It makes the game feel less epic to go from 23VP to 40VP during a game. I'd like the game to be more tactical, but there are many turns you can't do what you want because you don't have the cards, so you just punt, and wait until the next turn. That's a problem with many deck-builders, though, so I won't fault this game for that. My favorite thing about the game is chaining together cards to move; you need a destination card, a transportation card, and sometimes a settler card and/or a boat. It makes it exciting when you can pull that move off. And then you have to factor in the usefulness of using a card with military strength when you want to save it for expansion, and you have decent tension there. Overall, I like this game, especially when it plays in 60 minutes or less.

RATING: 7
What other game have I played 26 times before rating? It would be impossible to rate A Few Acres of Snow after just one or two, but over the 26 I feel I've gone through various phases, from lack of understanding, to fake mastery, to learned ignorance, and I'm not sure where I've ended up. It's a fascinating game, really, combining aspects of tight deckbuilding, the location-and-other card system of Brass, and a 2p wargame. Definitely worth playing, and more than once or twice, but I've reached the point that it's no fun to play against my betters.

RATING: 10
There is a minor rules tweak by Wallace in the rules forum that addresses a balance issue.

This game TOTALLY ROCKS.

Hands down, the best two player game I have ever played. Experienced players can finish in about an hour. 

It's deep, yet not overly complex, and, like most Wallace games, the theme is so seamlessly integrated with the mechanics, you get the feeling that the most effective way to win is to transport yourself to the time and place.

I hope it inspires other designers to take deck building in new directions. Dominion, Thunderstone, etc. are now dead to me. Okay, not dead, I still like them a lot, but they're appetizers. This game is the meal. 

Like the scene in Ratatouille when the critic eats the dish that changes the way he thinks about food - this game raises the bar for deck building and two player games in general.

Update after many online games on Yukata. Still my favorite 2 player game. Just keeps getting better. Perfect blend of strategy and tactics. Still by far the best use of the "deck building" mechanic.

The only way to make this game better is to give me an iPad version.

Update after even more plays. This is still my favorite game. So much better than the fussy, overwrought, overly-long, Twilight Struggle in the deeper 2 player game category. As far as I'm concerned, there is AFAOS, and then everything else.


Regarding the Halifax Hammer - I don't know anybody who actually plays the game that considers it a problem. As of now, I've played 145 times on Yucata; it's a completely overblown non-issue and a friggin' shame that one of the best games of all time was maligned from the start by a bunch of naysayers who never even played, otherwise they'd realize that the only time you would ever go through the rote, scripted, exploit is to prove it's broken. 

Again... other than to prove that the exploit exists, there is no reason to ever do it. The other player would know immediately what you were trying to do, and what you'd be trying to do is prove a point - the point being, you're a jerk who just wasted a half hour instead of playing a game. Also, after the first two turns, if you don't pursue it step by step, precisely and by rote, it won't work. 

The one time I executed a Halifax Hammer, I literally sat there with the script of how to do it that I'd printed out, paying careful attention to each move - it was not unlike the time I time I had heard that if you play Dark Side Of The Moon to coincide with the beginning of The Wizard Of Oz it matches up; kind of a weird novelty I had to try myself to see if it actually worked... definitely not something I'd ever feel compelled to do more than once. So here's how to "fix" the problem - don't do it!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I'm not a big fan of Dominion et al but this game uses the deck-building mechanic in an interesting and unique way. Capture of locations plus payment for cards allows you to build your deck. The inherent time delay in putting the card into the deck and actually drawing the card nicely simulates the difficulties of conducting a campaign with such long supply lines. There would appear to be multiple routes the victory and the key would seem to establish a strategy and build a deck accordingly (do you choose all-out aggression or settlement or a combination of the two) If your deck becomes too big you can get bogged down by not being able to draw the right card combos, too small and you have too few options. This is an intriguing came with lots to explore.

RATING: 6.2
Like After the Flood, faintly disappointing. It has all the hallmarks of a Wallace game: innovative ideas, tons of theme, interesting mechanics. But the Dominion-reshuffle idea makes the game take far too long and breeds frustration. Call me crazy, but I don't find it fun to churn and churn for cards that allow me to do what I could do in five minutes in a wargame. If Wallace was going to hijack this idea (I'm glad he did, I loathe Dominion), he needed to improve the boring, staid parts of it. Why play this or Dominion when you've got Ascension and/or Quebec 1759?

RATING: 7
I wasn't sure what to expect with A Few Acres of Snow, but I came away pleasantly surprised. It is definitely a war game with a deck-building engine at its core. It can be a little daunting at first, because there are literally like 15 different things you can do on your turn with your two actions, and between that and the huge pool of cards you can add to your deck, it's a lot to take in. It's very likely that my rating will rise as I play this more, but I'm not sure I have the time to devote to something with such deep strategy.

UPDATE: Yeah, I just don't have the time to sink my teeth into this one. It's a good game, even if the designer has admitted to it being broken. I'm not sure I like Wallace's take on deck building, as a mechanism, anyway. It felt like his deck building games got progressively worse following this one.

RATING: 8
I first got this back in 2012, loved it, and almost immediately plunged into the community effort to "fix" it.
Though I no longer own a physical copy, I continue to play online, and have probably played about 100 games, and continue to enjoy it and regard it as one one of my favorites.

Many people consider the game "broken" but I think it is more accurate to say "solved." And it is really only solved at the very highest standard of play. My judgment of the game has matured over time, and the problem of a dominant strategy seems less of an issue to me than it once did.

Enjoy the game. It's a lot of fun. If you ever get to the point where you feel you can no longer lose playing British you can start introducing variant rules (especially No Governor on starting locations, which I believe comes very close to "fixing" the problem).

Don't miss out on a great game on account of the fact that there are players out there who can always win. 

All that said, if they ever come out with a 3rd edition, that has some kind of variable set up, or officially incorporates elements of the Random Rules Generator, I would absolutely buy it.

RATING: 9.5
   This is quite different from other Martin Wallace games i have played. I was worried whether Martin can design a war game well before  I play this game.But finally Mr Wallace surprise me a again.You can hardly figure out how can DBG and wargame combined so good, but Martin make it!

    For sure, this is not a "real-wargame(you know what I mean)",but for most non-wargamers,A Few Acres of Snow givs them an impression about what war is like --- you need to prepare well to win a siege, or sometimes you want to support a town besieged but you are too late,etc.

    The game play is also addicting,fast pace,high tense You have dozens of thing to do but you can only do two.This is a game i'll play it several time is a single day, very few games can make me do that.

    The only thing that does not satisfy me is that, comparing with other games like brass or AoS, A Few Acres of Snow is not deep enough ---- but still plenty of strategy, so it still has high replaybility.  

PS:The game weight should be 3.4 or heavier, maybe the clear written rules make people think it is simpler than it should be .

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This game simulates the 18th century battles between Britain and France for control of what is now North America. In this card driven game, the mechanics are borrowed from Dominion, but it does it to realistically simulate how wars were fought then. If you need resources, you need to make a request for them, and here they show up in your discard pile before being part of your draw deck. 

You can settle locations with a village, or upgrade them to double your VPs. You also score points by capturing cubes of your opponents through seiges or raids. 
The game can also end if either side captures a key town. 

This is a very good game, and I am looking forward to more plays. There are many strategies to pursue, and many things to do! Too bad it only plays with 2 players.

RATING: 7
A VERY different deck building game. This isn't for those casual gamers that love Dominion or Ascension. This reminds me more of a game for the fans of Twilight Struggle or 1960. RATING: 6
I have really been digging A Few Acres of Snow; I think it's a very innovative system. I have been introducing it as "Dominion, where estates are actually useful". Pretty much all of my games except my first have been games where I was teaching it to someone new, so the actual games have been a foregone conclusion, and more of a demo game than the man to man intellectual combat I crave.

The current dominant strategies for both sides have all revolved around deck thinning, followed by building up a large bank, splurging on military and going to war. I expect that taking any of the game-ending locations would be extremely difficult against experienced opponents. 

This game reminds of Through the Ages: A Story of Civilization in that military is not required... unless your opponent goes military. In a game where both players go the military route, it will be interesting to see who has the advantage.

---

The core of the game is pretty simple. It's a deck-building wargame. But that description belies the intricate dance of delicious strategic possibilities that rarely seems possible in anything other than an RTS computer game against a competent and human opponent. Because of the deck-building nature of the game, even the seemingly simplest decisions have long-term strategic consequences that you will rue for turns afterward, with only the occasional raid/ambush tactical decision giving you some relief.

It's still in discovery mode, meaning I can't be sure at any given point what the best move is for either side -- is rock better than paper or scissors?* Unfortunately, my most reliably likely opponent is not quite as passionate about the game as I am, and I may never have the opportunity to completely spelunk the wondrous depths I remain convinced this game opens up to its devotees.

---

Okay. Rating heavily downgraded. The game as published has serious balance issues: British can easily force a win. We can balance the game with some heavy rules modifications, but this exposes an even worse core gameplay weakness: The optimal strategy in the game is essentially fixed. Unless we also houserule some variability to the empire decks or the starting locations, the optimal path just plays itself out, with only minor decisions required. (Imagine Dominion with only a single set of 10 kingdom cards.)

Dominion is like Lake Pontchartrain: Very shallow. You can figure out a given set after 1-2 plays, but there is so much of it, that you could swim around it forever without seeing same thing twice.

Chess and Go are Lake Baikal: Extremely deep. You can see across it, but you can never swim to the bottom.

AFAoS is like my community pool.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A true hybrid game that combines wargame-like forward planning and supply chain management with eurogame-like card manipulation, deck-building style.

I have played several times and so far consider this to be one of the finest efforts of 2011. It's also far and away my favourite Martin Wallace game since Automobile.

If this is what deck-building can result in, then I'm happy Dominion was invented, because it allowed games like these to come about. No, this isn't Dominion - if you want a 20 minute exercise in figuring out the 'right' card combination first, go away. But if you want an engaging struggle that finely meshes tactics and strategy and provides multiple potential paths to victory, then do yourself a favour and get this game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
規則:
*深色牌為各自起始牌組洗勻抽五張，淺色牌兩方分別將地區牌跟帝國牌分開放圖板對應區，中立牌則放一旁，法國(藍)起始5圓，英國(紅)12圓，在各自圖板上有顏色的區域擺上對應的方塊或圓片
*遊戲輪流進行，第一輪雙方都一個行動，之後都兩個，英國方起始，每次輪到時先確認是否攻城成功在繼續行動，結束時手牌補到五張，不夠補棄牌洗勻在補，可選行動如下
***擴張相關***
>開墾 出一張相鄰目標沒方塊地點的地區牌跟一張相連該兩點的交通工具牌(看牌下方，河流要木筏，海要船，陸地要馬車)，如果該地點有移民標記要再出一張移民牌，之後放方塊在該地點，並從自己的地點牌庫中找到該張地區牌放到棄牌區，出的牌都棄掉
>發展 出該地區卡跟一張移民卡，把方塊換成圓片，有分數的地點才行，之後分數兩倍
>堡壘 出一張防禦工事牌跟該地區牌，支付3圓，放一個黑色圓片在該地點方塊或圓片下方，一地區最多一個，不會被掠奪，且防禦時戰力加2
*如果該區域卡上沒有你的標記或是該地區無法連到(印地安鐵路以外的連線)你的起始主城(波士頓或魁北克)或是該區域被圍城，則該地區卡不能使用，所有有船符號的地點都相鄰，同湖泊的也相鄰
***攻擊相關***
>發動圍城  出一張相鄰目標地點的地區牌跟連結的交通工具卡(出完棄掉)，放戰爭標記在蓋地點，然後出戰力牌放在圖板對應戰爭區(放在攻擊方的區域)，同時調整戰力軌，也要放在對應戰力軌，先根據守方基礎戰力(什麼都沒有防禦力1，有符號+1，堡壘+2)跟你出的戰鬥牌來調整
*海港戰鬥時 船可當一點攻擊力
*每個玩家回合一開始如果戰力軌在你的顏色區域則勝利，攻擊方勝利取走該地區圓片或方塊，防禦工事移除，蓋地區有移民符號則要出移民牌才能放方塊，否則直接放，並新增地區牌到棄牌庫，可選擇不放，戰鬥結束時勝力方所有戰鬥牌到棄牌堆，失敗方要放回一張地區牌以外的牌，其他才到棄牌堆
>追加戰力 直接出有戰力相關的牌一張到戰爭區，同時調整戰力軌
*防禦工事卡  防守方可出可戰力+1，但不能再被打的地方建造堡壘
*軍事領袖 免費行動 戰力+1
>掠奪 出1到多張掠奪卡，攻擊距離相鄰你出張數+1的對方地區(從你的地區出發)，每張原住民卡要出一圓，路徑可包含印地安小徑但不能經過海港，但不能越過或掠奪有堡壘的地方，宣告後對手可出手牌來阻擋不需花錢(擋掠奪的或是該地區牌)，掠奪成功拿走該地區的方塊或圓片(拿走圓片對方放方塊)
>突襲 打出突襲卡啟動對手要失去一張保留區或手牌有突襲符號的牌，對手可出對應牌阻擋突襲，失去的牌放回對應供應區
>佈道招降  出牌奪取保留區或對手手上的原住民卡，成功放到自己的棄牌堆
*priest indian leader也可增加掠奪距離，但單獨出不能發起掠奪
****金錢相關***
>稅收 打出下方有錢符號的拿錢
>貿易 打一張有大船的並且打出1~2張有錢符號的拿錢
>買賣皮毛 出交易牌，然後同時出多張有皮毛的卡，每張兩元
>海盜 打路易斯柏格地區牌跟一張船卡，從對手那拿兩元，不夠拿從銀行補
***管理動作***
>買帝國牌 支付左上金額拿牌到棄牌堆
>棄牌 超過一張之後每張要一元
>保留牌 出一張到保留區，保留區上限5張
>拿回  一次拿回所有保留區的牌，每張一圓
>執政官 從手牌把1~2張牌放回對應的供應區
>總督 付兩元，從棄牌堆拿一張牌到手上
>母國支援 免費行動，抽三張牌，補牌時該牌不洗入
***其他動作***
>撤退 免費行動，撤退者視為落敗結算
>跳過 單純不行動
*英國打下魁北克或是法國打下波士頓或紐約直接勝利(打贏就好不用放方塊)
*如果玩家回合開始時沒戰爭，且有人放完所有方塊跟圓片，或是有人擄走對方14分的指示物(方塊2，圓片4)，遊戲結束，計算圖板有分數的地點(圓片乘2)，拿走對方的方塊2分，圓片4分，分數高勝利，平手法國勝利

RATING: 8
A really nice deck building twist with lots of strategic decisions to make on how you build your deck - settler heavy to build cities for VPs, troop heavy to inflict sieges to steal cities, raider heavy to steal VPs, deck manipulation cards, defence cards, and so on. As your deck grows, you get good turns and poor turns, simulating the ebb and flow of the conflict. It doesn't feel like a war game, more of expansion and competition for restricted spaces, with multiple ways to choose to do it. Lots to explore. I like it.

RATING: 9
You build a deck and then use it to perform actions. You can buy cards into your deck, but also obtain city cards by exploring or conquering regions on the board. The game is a mix of card drafting and a battle for location on the board. When besieging your opponent, you must continue the battle from round to round until one player holds the lead for a full turn.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I love the concept of this game and like the deck-building, especially how unforgiving it is. You have to be very selective about what sort of strategy you take as having a deck with too many cards can leave with totally useless cards (discarding costs money and a precious action!). I really enjoy the location mechanic that makes you play one location card to get to another, then also have to play the type of transportation to get there, inspired! 

The only issue I have with the game is that due to the way the map is drawn it’s hard to know which locations you need to get to other ones, especially the locations that are connected via ship.

If you’re looking into getting this but you’re worried about the Halifax Hammer, as long as you don’t look into the specifics of that strategy then you’ll be fine. I’ve played this a few times with people who know of the strategy’s existence, but we couldn’t figure it out, so I don’t think it is the most obvious and that allows you to play the game without it being broken.

RATING: 7
Very interesting war/deckbuilding hybrid, with a heavy economic development aspect too.

This game has a lot of rules and options (probably not compared to other wargames, but it surprised me); there are many ways in which you can combine the cards in your hand, and then there are a lot of different cards to buy.

Like Brass, I felt that in AFAoS cards limit more what you can do then give you options. There's 20+ things you can do, but only if you have a card with a specific symbol on it. I really dislike that in games. As the game goes on, you can buy more cards that let you do cool stuff, but it still feels somewhat 'off'.

I love the idea, and think the design is great, but ultimately, I don't think it's a game for me. The many ways to win, the lack of direction the game gives you, the overwhelming number of options when you first start playing, being at the mercy of the cards and the balance issues mean I can't really see myself enjoying this with my friends.

There really is a lot to explore here, which is great, but that sadly doesn't fit my gaming habits, and AFAoS doesn't seem like a game for the occasional 2-player wargamer to me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
It's a shame that this game became the lynchpin of an ideological war, as it never set out to be a representative of such theoretical discussion and critique. If you don't know what I'm referring to, search "Halifax Hammer" for a far better explanation than I can provide.

Having followed the above-mentioned tempest in a teapot with detached curiosity, I was intrigued at the chance to actually play the game in question. With no knowledge of the intricacies of Nova Scotian bluntness, I sat to play as the French against my friend, who owned the game and played as the Brits. He apparently had never heard about the hammer either, so we played an old-fashioned slugfest that probably looked a lot like the way the designer envisioned.

And it was great.

Yeah, the rules were kinda tough to remember and complex in certain areas, but as a somewhat abstract wargame, there is really no way to avoid it. This game was made for players who crave that type of complexity, and it delivers (even though it's not my favorite type of game, it fulfills its intended purpose so I can't complain). The game had tons of crunch, and there were meaningful decisions to be made every turn. It flavorfully simulates the bureaucracy of fighting a war, and there are plenty of interesting ways to manage your resources. If you want to play this to have an experience and not to rise to top-level greatness, it's fine. If you want to talk about the problems at the highest level, go ahead, but for 90+% of all players, that won't ever matter. Accept it for what it is, and it's a beautiful game.

And now, a word about the designer: There was a certain recorded instance in which he responded very poorly to criticism and took potshots at some of his fans. Since then, he seems to have mellowed out and accepted criticism more gracefully. You shouldn't let that affect your perception/enjoyment of the game. You might not like him (I'm certainly not his biggest fan after what he said), but don't let that cloud your image of his work. It stands on his own.

Update: Since I've started rating the designers in some of these, I'll give Martin Wallace a 5/10 for refusing to admit he made a minor error and instead whining incessantly before mellowing out a bit. Doesn't affect my rating of the game though.

RATING: 3
Disclaimer: I do not like deck building games nor do I like Wallace's designs. I played half a game with someone who has similar tastes as I do.

I do like the theme, though.

We found this game exceedingly slow and lacking in any tension. Later in the game, we figured out what we should have done in order to manage our decks better. Instead, we decided to abandon the game for something more interesting.

It could be argued that I should try this again; the game should play much faster. However, I don't have any interest in doing so. This is a deck building game with a pasted-on theme. I do not feel like I am fighting a war between the French and the British. I feel like I am managing a deck. For example, movement of units to settle or attack is over-abstracted to waiting for a very specific card combination. When I play a war game, I want at least the geography of the map and the manoeuvring of units to have a significant role in the game. In this game, it's all about managing a deck of cards, to which many of the strategy guides here attest.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Seeing balanced games now: 54/53 last game. Don't listen to the excitable ultra-competitive types, this game has legs.

=====================================================

Nifty marriage of deck management and territorial expansion game. And unlike most of Wallace's euro/conflict hybrids, A Few Acres seems robust and resilient.

Presents tough choices of several strategic options (expansion, raiding, sieging, development), and rewards specialization enough that you need to have a plan, but not so much that it's impossible to adjust. My only concern is that all of our games so far have been lopsided. I can discern how we will change tack to counter one another as we become more experienced, but I haven't seen a tight, back-and-forth sort of game at the table so far.

A Few Acres of Snow is something of a breakthrough in wargames, along the lines of We the People. However, my guess is (like WtP) it will be superceded by more sophisticated and polished iterations of the system.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I almost peed when Martin Wallace gave me a free copy of this at Essen 2011, after I gave him a copy of JAB to thank him for allowing me to do the game designer promo.  Felt like 2 soccer players trading jerseys.  Except he's Christiano Ronaldo, and I'm some nobody brought up from the minors.

Update:  Just played my 8th game, and still peeling back the many layers of this giant onion.  If I could give this game an 11, I would. Martin Wallace is my hero.

9th Play: One of the most intense gaming experiences I've ever had.  I was holding off the British quite well along the coast with many many fortifications, but had to sacrifice interior defenses to do so.  I was winning in points until I failed to fend off a final siege on Fort Frontenac.  This game plays like it took 100 years to develop.

After 40 games or so, many of which were losing to the HH against out4blood, my rating of 10 remains.  It takes an amazing game to get me to play it 40 times these days, and this one still has my attention.  I hope Martin fixes the game, as it is probably my favorite game of all time (second of course, to Brass).  I feel it is of paramount importance to Martin Wallace to fix this game (properly), as it clearly represents a potential masterpiece that will stand for years and years and years to come as one of the best war games ever created.

Current hypotheses on fixing the game:
Player decks should almost never drop below 11-12 cards in order for this game to function as it is intended to.  Sadly, the last rule change of "don't allow reserving location cards", while at first glance sounds like a good solution to increase British deck size, has an adverse effect (damaging the French more than the British).  This is because the French can no longer reserve locations that are under siege, resulting in them having to a) discard besieged location cards b) governor them away, which sucks if they actually win the siege, or c) let them rot in their hand reducing their hand size by 1, which is horrifying.  Not being able to reserve locations also makes fortifying, governoring and developing locations (2 primary focuses of the French), more difficult and random.  

The change to home support is genius, and actually makes card flow MUCH more important and strategic.  Also, because of home support's cost early game, it is no longer an auto buy (a good thing).

The fundamental problem with the British is that their starting cards are far too multi use.  With merely 3 of the british starting cards, you can develop locations, trader for 6, reenforce a seige, access key points on the map, and settle a location after winning a siege.  You can do EVERYTHING.  This means you require little to no cooldown between winning a siege and beginning the next one, and your money engine represents the same set of cards as all the cards required to settle.  Because of the size of your deck, you also only need to wait 1-2 turns between purchasing and reenforcing a siege, VS the french who have to wait 2-4 turns between buying and reenforcing (especially when they are defending in a siege)

RATING: 7
06-03-2015.Rated after 1 play. Frontrunner for Mythotopia, this game plays 2 and is thematic. Mythotopia uses a generic theme to accomodate the 4p.

Both games share many mechanisms. They are deckbuilders in which you draw 5 cards and use your cards to build fortifications, attack cities and settle locations.

Both games treat war in the same way, you buy troop cards which you use a tug-of-war like mechanism to see who wins. at the start of each turn players can end combat, but only if you are winning. 

I like the game a lot.

RATING: 9
I'm hesitant to rate this yet with only a couple plays under my belt, because I see a staggering amount of strategic depth I have not yet begun to delve into. Even though the deck building is an integral part of gameplay, I think A Few Acres of Snow lands firmly in the light war game genre. Rule explanation can be lengthy at 45-60 min, and I think that alone may scare off the casual Dominion crowd.

The artwork is great, albeit confusing when it comes to figuring out where you can raid from, but everything else seems well-balanced and functional. apart from a slight learning curve due to the mechanics of the cards, I could see this being a great introduction into light wargaming, along with the likes of Memoir 44 and Battle Cry.

I am thoroughly impressed by this deck-building war game hybrid by Martin Wallace.

RATING: 3
AFAoS shows the way that deckbuilding games need to go (for me at least): positional play with a board that creates direct confrontation between two opponents.

Unfortunately, in this case the concept is poorly executed: as the designer himself admits, this game is flawed. 

At first, AFAoS seems to have a dizzying, overwhelming variety of options and its level of innovation seems staggering. However, you quickly realise that there are far fewer paths to victory than at first appear, and then you hear about the all-conquering Halifax Hammer. It may be difficult to pull off, but the game is fundamentally broken as if both players play to win, the game will always more or less go down the same route. 

Some can  "forget" the optimal strategy and  enjoy the experience of the game. I can't. To dismiss those who enjoy playing the game with the goal of winning as anally retentive min-max optimisers is a silly caricature used to defend a fundamentally flawed product. Indeed, if you don't play with a thin deck, the game gets rather boring as both players end up spending ages going through their deck looking for the card combination they need to do anything.

There are plenty of robust two-player games out there that tell a narrative yet hold up to competitive play. Why would I ever choose to play AFAoS over one of those?

Traded for Mythtopia (mistake!).

RATING: 7
The controversy surrounding this game is instructive, and an important discussion. It seems a shame though that so many people who haven't actually played the game have written it off as broken as a result. For me, a neat game, and one of the first really great games to follow Dominion using a deck-building mechanism - more importantly the first board game to successfully fuse deck building into its play - My opinion, of course, with little regard for the dissenters. I haven't played enough to run in to the Halifax Hammer - if I did, or if I generally found deck-thinning overpowered, I'd probably follow Mantuanwar's suggested variant rather than Mr Wallace's proposed fix - but it hasn't come up, so we've just played RAW. It's a really beautiful production too, if you find any appeal in the Treefrog aesthetic, and represents its chosen period of history beautifully.   
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A very hard game to find an appropriate rating for at the moment. After being somewhat sceptical the game has been demonstrated to me to be either broken or so close to it that it's splitting hairs. (A rough form of this strategy was being played by us before it dawned on us that this was the best way for the British to play. Only later did it acquire a name and a brain free process of enactment.) This ruins the enjoyment somewhat of an otherwise very fine game. Players wanting a fun game try to stay clear of the offending strategy, but it feels like there's an elephant in the room. I've dropped my rating down to a six (not broken, but based on my desire to keep playing a game where I have to ignore this elephant), where I expect it will remain until a fix is provided by the designer that restores the challenge and fun to the game. The 2nd edition "fix" is NOT that fix.

The only fix I've found for the Halifax Hammer is to play with someone who agrees they won't play it. The Brits can still win by taking Quebec, but they can't take it by playing in a way resembling the HH. Alternatively not allowing the governor to offload starting towns (unless those towns have been captured), can also be fun even if the game is now most likely to be won by the Brits, but not guaranteed.
------------
There is a danger playing with inexperienced players that they might discard location cards that will then lead the game to get stuck in a rut as they waste turns to reacquire them. Either warn them this can happen, or implement a house rule for newbies that players can't discard location cards until they've been turned into cites or been expanded from. I've been in a game where it's gummed up and it's no wonder players are unimpressed by the game when this happens.

Reading the rules I was sceptical. How was this going to work? It seemed a bit hopeful to build a deck building mechanic into a themed game and not come away with an abstract feeling game pasted onto a serious wargame. Well, it is all a bit abstract, but through small differences between the British and French decks it retains enough of an historical simulation to pull it off. And does it ever succeed in pulling it off! As I abandon more and more the abstract games from my precious gaming time, this game gives me that themed game feeling with a card play system that is different for wargames and really works to provide me with an entertaining game.

What strikes me is how many choices a player has to approach the game. Say you're playing the French. Do you attempt to build up your siege troops, or push your settlements out for a quick end to the game? Geographically where are you going to spread out? Should you play raid cards into your reserve readying for ambushes and assaults, or do you try and build them up in your hand for a series of surprise attacks. (Surely your opponent can't block you twice in a turn, or can they?) What's your opponent doing in their reserve? Surely you'd better react, but you want to build up a useful combination of cards that's not just going to block your hand up for the next turn.

All the time you're paying attention to the little things like how many cards do you have left in your draw pile. How many does your opponent? Try and select the optimal time to buy, the best time to raid a particular settlement or town.

Then play the other side and it's a completely different style of play with different quirks to consider. The French with their lack of settlement cards. The British weaker in trading income. The French with their more useful militia. The British with their ship income.

Okay, this is a very simple to play game that'll give me many, many plays before I'll get tired of it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
Man, do I hate this game. Got halfway through and gave up. 

You need combinations of cards in order to do anything. You start with cards that don't seem very good, but a few of them are versatile. As you add cards to your deck, they are generally only used for one thing, making your deck even streakier. You can spend multiple turns cycling through your deck in order to actually be able to do something. This may be thematic, but it is not fun. The military and raiding actions are unsatisfying, and the game plays way too slowly. Actually none of the actions you can perform are satisfying, but the military actions stand out as being particularly unsatisfying. 

It was pretty clear by the time I gave up on the game that the only way to play well is to use the card that removes cards from your deck to make your deck efficient. In order to do that well, you would need to analyze all of the cards for your faction ahead of time to see what you should remove. Doing so removes most of the decision making from the game, and requires you to perform a tedious analysis before playing. 

It's such a cool idea, but the implementation is horrible in practice.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
It's not merely that this game is broken, but also that Martin Wallace seems resigned to the fact that you have to sacrifice balance for asymmetry and thematic authenticity (as seen in Brass, which also has unintended strategies, but is held together by having no asymmetry), and that asymmetric 2-player games are impossible to balance. That's not true. Maybe he can get David Sirlin to help with development.

Even if the game weren't broken, it's lackluster for me. AFAoS's claim to fame is "board-izing" Dominion, and slapping on a theme that makes sense (hiring mercenary forces takes a while for them to come over from Europe, so the card goes in your deck, not hand). But it's disappointing to me that the board doesn't feel like a board sometimes (some locations look like they should be adjacent, but then when you look at the actual cards, it says they're not, or only adjacent for one of the players). And the biggest letdown for me was the menu of options on your turn. There is a bevy of things you can do for your turn, but most of these are just formalized extensions of Dominion's many cards (chain, acquire, thin, cycle, build). The end-result is nothing that feels groundbreaking, and is too clunky and weird compared to Dominion's simple, streamlined system.

I'm not a fan of Wallace-style menu of action options for your 2 actions a turn. Feels like lazy game design to me.

3+1

Update 5/13 -- British need to play a bit more like a wargame and France needs to play a bit more like an empire expansion game. Building and maintaining an effective deck may be the most important and most difficult part of playing this game well.

Most recent play Jan '16.

Kétszemélyes háborús eurogame. Pakliépítős, Londonra hajazó csak más körítésben. Francia - angol párharcról szól a játék története az Észak-Amerika feletti uralomért. A város és faluhelyek fixek a játékban, nekem a fő gondom főleg ezzel volt, hogy nem érzek benne sok játékot, mert az igaz, hogy a kártyák miatt minden parti más, de adott kártyákból egyértelmű mi a legjobb akcióválasztás. Nagyon sok akciólehetőség van, de nagyon könnyű kizárni a gyengéket a lapok láttán. A harcrendszer jól ki van találva, többféleképpen lehet megszerezni egymás falu és városjelző korongjait. A játék végét kiváltó egyik feltétel valamelyik fél falu vagy városkorongjainak elfogyása. Szabály alapján is nekem egyértelműnek tűnt erre a stratégiára rámenni, háború nélkül csak fejlődni és működött. Nem tudom mennyire zavaró lehet egy háborús játékban csata nélkül nyerni, de nekem kicsit fura volt. Összességében jó téma, sokféleképpen variálható pakli, de mégsem érzek benne sok játéklehetőséget és a hódítás sem túl kifizetődőnek látszik elsőre. RATING: 8
This game introduces a brilliant idea in combining deck-building with a campaign map. Plays quite smoothly, and does what many wargames fail to do via incorporating the logistical, political, and economic downsides of a military campaign. For example, there's a certain paranoia involved when the French start recruiting Indian allies for raids forcing the British to clog their deck with fortifications and counter-raids reducing the effectiveness of both their economic and military engines. I also enjoy the asymmetrical aspects of the game with the massive British colonies on one side and the rapidly expanding inland French setttlements on the other. I do think the game just about misses out on greatness because a few aspects are not fully worked out. Seems to me that the British can too easily overwhelm the French along the coast.

RATING: 7.5
A great game -- deck-building with an actual game attached to it! It's a wargame, though; not my usual choice in games. However, it's simple enough that, at least against another wargame neophyte, I can probably do sort of OK. 

Some official rule changes to try (and FAIL) to fix the broken Halifax Hammer strategy from the original game:
1. Remove the French bateaux card from the initial deck and place it with the availble cards.

2. Reserve - you cannot place location cards in your reserve.

3. Raids - the raid distance is now two connections plus one connection for each additional card played (so playing two Native American cards would allow you to raid a location up to three connections away).

4. Home Support - you can only take cards from your draw deck, so if you had fewer than three cards available you would only be able to draw these. Consequently, the only time you shuffle your discard pile is when you need to refill your hand at the end of the turn and your draw deck is exhausted.

This seems to be a great system! Unfortunately, I just can't seem to figure out how to play the French competitively. And the British seem to have one main path: thin the deck, buy military, and crush the French! After several plays, it's getting to be more frustrating than interesting!

After initially rating it 8, I have to drop the rating a bit ... there's just too much emphasis on the Halifax Hammer. But it's still such a neat game!

After playing a bit with the new random rules generator, rating crawling up a bit to 7.5 for now.

--- Random Rule Generator ---
Before the game, determine 3 different rules by rolling two dice (first for group, second for rule).

GROUP 1-2
1) Pay 1 coin when reshuffling your deck (if you cannot, discard 2 cards after refilling the hand to 5).
2) One fortification disc in Louisburg during setup.
3) French instant win if he builds a city in Fort Duquesne.
4) If you do not block an ambush, remove a vulnerable card from your hand, reserve or siege (in the last case, modify siege track accordingly).
5) 3 Regular Infantry cards are removed from the English Empire (they cannot be bought).
6) French player can discard a card from his hand and draw a new card whenever a siege is resolved (before taking any action).

GROUP 3-4
1) Locations cards can be put in Reserve.
2) English player adds "Boat" card to his starting deck.
3) A blocked ambush is not considered an action.
4) A blocked pillage is not considered an action.
5) One fortification disc in Quebec during setup.
6) Both players can play a card with a "Fur" as a free action to gain 1 coin.

GROUP 5-6
1) When you discard, you may discard how many cards you like (for free).
2) French player adds any one card from his Empire to his starting deck.
3) If you put a Location card back into the available cards, then you lose control of that location.
4) Natives have a military power of 1 and can be used during sieges.
5) English player cannot start a siege until the French player starts one OR he has 25 Victory Points.
6) If you lose control of a location, you may remove its card from your hand as a free action.

RATING: 4
I will admit that my dislike of this game after 1 play may be a bit unfair.  I found myself in what is possibly an uncommon situation and that ruined the game for me but I'm willing to give this one another shot to see if there was something there that was robbed from me in that first play.

This is a deck builder with some war game elements.  There is a static map setup based on some historic war and the cards you play each turn determine your expansion and siege of different areas of the map.

Now let me explain where this game went horribly, horribly wrong in my first play.  I played the French side and my opponent took a location in my area on the very first turn so I decided to try to take that location back early before he had an opportunity to entrench himself.  Now I'm not a war game player and I don't even win that often playing Risk Legacy with my usual group so maybe this was a simple mistake in game play but what ensued really spoiled this game for me.

First, I don't know that I could have won because I played it well (in my non-war gamer opinion anyway) and still lost.  I bolstered my attack each turn and spent my money on new units to bolster it even more but every progress I made was easily taken down by the British player.  He started with a smaller deck and much more money so was able to cycle through it, buy more useful cards, and get to them faster than me and eventually (more inevitably) won the battle.  Meanwhile I spent resources crowding my deck with military units but not the transportation cards needed to get to the locations I needed to attack.

The deck makeup for the French player doesn't seem as good as the British.  I only started with 2 ship symbols and literally no location in the available deck had ship symbols on it and it would have cost an action and 6 gold to buy just a ship card (which is much more inefficient than the multiple ship symbols and available locations that also have other symbols and uses in the British deck).  Also the money-making potential for the French is the trader card which while possibly very lucrative (Trader + 4 fur symbols = 8 money and empties your hand), with just a single trader in the starting deck, it is hard to get to it.  There is also a similar situation with settlers which I believe the British starts with more of (The French only start with 1) and even if the British don't start with more their smaller deck makes them easier to get to anyway.

After my initial attempt at assault I found myself constantly drawing hands of cards where I literally had no good options, especially now with tons of military units in my deck and no ways to get to the locations I would want to attack.  You don't discard your hand at the end of a turn in this game so if you have a bad hand you have to spend actions AND money to discard.  If you are out of money like I found myself then you have to spend both your actions to discard just two cards from your shitty hand.  I found myself doing this 3 turns in a row before I had had it and quit the first game I can ever remember quitting without even finishing it due to having so little fun.

It is awful to spend 3 turns going "I discard two cards, go" and watch your opponent increase his board position each time and knowing you're falling further and further behind.

Like I said, I would play it again to see if this was a fluke but my gut is telling me because of some of the design of the game (the British vs. the French deck and the no discard rule) and how awful things did go that this isn't really just a fluke...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5

I like unbalanced playing strategies (but only if all players have the same possibility of winning the game) and I have the feeling that FA of Snow is one of those balanced games with totally different strategies to follow when playing with France or England and that's not only very hard to accomplish but also very fun to see in a game.
Another good thing I enjoy a lot is that the game doesn't have a very remarkable war-game feeling too. Players settle a lot and develop a lot and make only a few sieges during the game. I think that raids are the most simple and (maybe) powerful warfare mechanic in A Few Acres of Snow but they don't represent that heavy "destroy something" feeling, which I like. It's kind of a war for wimps with a small civ feeling.
Still need to play it again a couple of times more to understand better how long does it take.

RATING: 8
I have played this twice as the French and loved every minute of the game. I suspect the rating can only go down, but what a joy to play.

This  Economic/military game is the perfect home for the build a deck, manage and play it mechanic. There appear to be multiple paths to victory for both sides, with a genuine ability to customize your deck - though there is enough interaction that players really need to heed the opponents deck building strategy. The asymmetrical strengths and goals of the combatants adds to the appeal. My favourite Wallace since Automobile. Any worries I had about the number of potential actions were dispelled on first play - they are not overwhelming, and are mainly determined by your hand so there might only be 3 or 4 choices each hand and turns come around very quickly. Whilst each hand determines play there can be a little more deliberation over the stragic direction of your deck, and on of the pleasurable agonies of the game is taking a quiet turn of no progress (for example discarding cards , to draw into a combo you want to play). all the while you are convinced your opponent is making better progress than you.

Another aspect of play is hand management to enable a continual cycle of cards - it can be easy to get stuck with a hand you cant play, if you don't have cards that work well together (Trader,Fur or ships, Colonists)

I hope this could become a template for future economic/wargames and don't see why it would not work multi player as well

Odds on to win IGA 2 player game 2011 - in fact id say the book was closed

RATING: 8
A really astounding design. I'm not usually a fan of deck-building games: the luck factor is frustrating; the shuffling is tedious. This one is something special. The reserve, the sieges, the location cards and the ambushes are all brilliant twists on deck management -- based on previous history, any one of those innovations would've been enough for someone to launch yet another lame Dominion clone; the fact that they're combined here with board mechanisms in a way that is both novel, strategic [i]and thematic[/i] is mindblowing.

As for how it plays, well, we cocked up the first game something shocking and spent close to three hours in a tedious war of fortification. By the end it was obvious how much we'd missed, and I very much look forward to future plays.

RATING: N/A
Una guerra combattuta al confine di due possenti imperi. Per oltre centocinquant’anni l’Inghilterra e la Francia condussero una lotta serrata per il dominio del Nord America. A migliaia di chilometri dalle loro terre natie, coloni e soldati dovettero far fronte a impenetrabili foreste, imprevedibili tribù americane e a distanze smisurate. Nonostante questi ostacoli, riuscirono a ingaggiare numerose aspre battaglie, e alla fine fu l’Inghilterra a reclamare il premio del Quebec. Pochi Acri di Neve è un gioco per due giocatori che vi consente di ricreare questa contesa. Con le vostre azioni potrete cambiare il corso della storia.

Pochi Acri di Neve fa uso di un approccio innovativo alle meccaniche di gioco, utilizzando una serie di carte per rappresentare i luoghi e la potenza delle forze militari. Con il procedere della partita, ogni giocatore amplia la sua serie di carte e aumenta la gamma di azioni a sua disposizione.

Le strategie da esplorare sono molteplici: la velocità con cui intendete ammassare le vostre forze, il coinvolgimento dei nativi americani, e la quantità di energie da dedicare all’economia. Il gioco non si limita ai semplici combattimenti: dovrete riuscire a colonizzare le vostre terre per avere una speranza di vittoria.

RATING: 7
Wallace has successfully taken the Dominion engine and made it into an actual game.  The mechanics of this game are fantastic to be sure.  I find a lot of flaw in the following ideas:

1. There are certain cards that either side MUST buy.  This makes the game feel sort of scripted.

2.  There's not a lot of conflict.

3.  This is a Euro-game, not a war game.

4.  At heart, this is a simple optimization game.

I think this is a fine game and I like it well enough.  I don't think this is as good as the majority of people do.  It's fine and I'd play it again, but it's not amazing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Card drafting game with a strong wargame element. It's obviously inspired by 'Dominion' and unlike the many other games that have taken the deck building mechanism and failed to improve on it, 'A Few Acres of Snow' does something radically different whereby you use your cards to control movements, development (economic and building) and battles, and in doing so becomes a quite magnificent game. It's a two player game set in the North American war between Britain and France and both sides have to employ a quite different strategy from one another i.e the British use greater finances and ability to settle areas whilst the French use Native American resources to ambush and raid. This is a genuinely intriguing game to play and certainly one of Martin Wallace's finest designs.

RATING: N/A
Incredible climate, very enjoyable. The game core is stolen from Dominion, but it's used here so smartly! The players have many options to manipulate their decks. And with asymmetric forces and a number of very interesting openings, the game replayability seems to be high. Moreover, the theme is tightly sewed to the mechanics. A very good design.

There is also an issue of a broken British strategy: "a. minimise you deck, b. get much gold, c. buy army and capture Port Royal (Halifax), Louisbourg and Quebec". While I think it's beatable in some cases, IMHO the game needs a slight correction. 
The most promising and recent solution has been proposed [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/7903369#7903369][u]here[/u][/url]: the Home Support cards are available only after a player captures an opponent disk. IMHO Louisbourg should also be fortified from the game start. I played three times with these two particular corrections and liked it much. 
Anyway, the game is really, really good despite this issue. 

Update: Official rules changes have been presented [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/8136728#8136728][u]here[/u][/url].

Number of plays: 28.

RATING: 8
Fascinating use of the deck-building mechanic in support of a wargame. The deck-building is used to abstract supplies, transportation, and support. Managing your deck often overshadows your position on the map, but I never lost track of the fact that I was playing a wargame where position mattered. The map and artwork are beautiful. The replayability seems high. Yes, I've heard about the "unbeatable" or "broken" strategy. But, for now, I can still enjoy the game as a glimpse of how deck-building can be used for more than just another Dominion clone.

RATING: 8
A Few Acres of Snow is pretty challenging, but not overwhelming. It really does play in an hour, which is great. it also plays very differently depending on whether players focus on building vs. raids vs. protracted sieges. All of this I like very much. It's a little disturbing that at least one and possible more strategies (by the British) are generally being acknowledged as unstoppable, therefore "breaking" the game. While that gives me pause, I haven't played this enough to have seen this in action ... the couple of games I've played have been close affairs. Suffice to say, I hope Martin Wallace comes out with revised rules that fix the problems that have been exposed. In the meantime, it's the old joke: "Doctor, it hurts when I move like this." "Well, don't do that." There's plenty to explore here without going down the broken path, and the journey is rewarding. A Few Acres of Snow successfully melds Dominion-like deck-building with Euro mechanics and a light war game theme. So far, I'm enjoying this one.

RATING: 6
First of all: the game gets a -1,5 penalty for its rulebook. Get the f..... structure right! If you have elements on the board explain them first instead of explaining details that are in context with the element and then one or two pages later the element. This is driving me mad,because until I read about the element-part, I think I overread something and start to flip forward and backward in pages. Also, how about a keyword glossary? 

Concerning the game itself: this take on deck-building is interesting, but the game seems unfinished. I know of course about the Halifax Hammer.To be precise: I know that it's there. I didn't read about it because I didn't want to spoil the game for me. I never play games to the extent where I'd be coming close to find a game-breaking strategy. But it's not the Halifax Hammer I'm talking about.

What I found more disturbing is the power of raids vs. actually besieging someone. Besieging is something you have to prepare by longhand and you need tons of cards without even knowing if it works out in the end.Raids are so supereasy to do. And they are probably even more so when played by the French but in this case the British player had almost all Native Americans to his hand. The British player never had to besiege and with all his cards with settle-ability the British player never besieged the French player in this game for once. And the French player, already pretty locked and without options to spread out could just sit there and gaze for an hour waiting for the evident (i.e. the British victory) to happen. The British player jsut settled from one place to the next, developed the villages he already had one by one and the french player had no chance whatsoever to interfere. 

Probably this was an unusual constellation, but speaking of balancing I don't know where the strengths of the French player are supposed to be in the beginning. It's not the money (even with the trader you won't come close to the financial power of the Brits) and it feels like you're locked in your starting position apart from the Nova Scotia peninsula. 
I'd have a few ideas on how to play the Fench differently the next time. I just don't see it happen anytime soon.

RATING: 8
Thanks, Santa! I think this one will see a lot of table time!

Update: I seem to adore this more than everyone else local -- if only Dano lived a little closer!

Ignore the epic threads about how allegedly broken the game is.  Unless you set out to break it, you're not going to just accidentally stumble across the Halifax Hammer.  What you'll probably do is play and enjoy several games, in which the English will win slightly more than the French, maybe.  Hey, they won the real thing -- I'm OK with a little bias.

One of the big strengths of this game is its asymmetry. I think this throws a lot of people, but it keeps it in touch with its theme.  No, France, you are unlikely to win a military victory.  That's OK!  You were unlikely to win one back then, too.

RATING: 8
The best, most engaging Maths homework I've ever played.

In seriousness, this is a great competitive two-player game, but be warned that it /will/ give you a headache. It's incredible at what it does, and that's simulating impenetrable bureaucracy. You don't fight your opponent so much as try (and fail) to coerce your deck into cooperating with you. Recommended to anyone that would sacrifice moment to moment "fun" for the intense thrill when your deck finally - finally - spits out the right combination of cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
Short in scope deck building, card management game with a well intergrated theme and a wargame set-up. 

It's well designed, allows for a plethora of strategies and tactical considerations and makes for a solid (if exceptionally limited) experience. The theme also works well. The game is very enjoyable while it lasts.

AfAoS is, among other things, a tactical wargame with only one scenario and almost no luck. That's a terrible combination, as all available strategies will "quickly" and inevitably be exausted until only one remains. The absense of luck ensures that every game from then on will play the same, as there is no significant random factor to affect a play midgame.

In short this game literally self destroys. Once it reaches a critical number of plays, the dominating strategy is finally found and the game loses ALL of its replayability. Depending on the player skill, enthusiasm and forum reading this might take anything between 0 and 100 plays to realise. By definition, this "near miss of a great game", is unfortunately broken.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A Few Acres of Snow is a two-player, card-driven game about the French and British conflict in North America.
The card-play contains a focus on a deck-building mechanic similar to Dominion, and like many card-driven war games, each card will have multiple uses. The players have to choose only one aspect of the card to use when it is played. Each space captured by a player will add another card to the capturing player's deck.
From the box description:
A war fought at the edge of two mighty empires. For over one hundred and fifty years Britain and France were locked in a struggle for domination of North America. Thousands of miles from their homes, settlers and soldiers were faced with impenetrable forests, unpredictable American tribes, and formidable distances. Despite these obstacles they were able to engage in bitter warfare, with the British ultimately taking the prize of Quebec. A Few Acres of Snow is a two-player game that allows you to recreate this contest. You can change the course of history by your decisions.
A Few Acres of Snow takes an innovative approach to the subject, using cards to represent locations and manpower. As the game progresses you add to your selection of cards, increasing the range of actions available to you. There are many strategies to be explored. How quickly should you build up your forces, do you employ Native Americans, what energy should be expended on your economy?
The game is about more than just fighting – you must successfully colonize the land to have a chance.

RATING: 8
Rating after two plays. So far, it seems that this is my favorite game from my Essen 2011 shopping list! Our first play took almost 3 hours, which proves that there is a lot of room to improve our deckbuilding strategies. Because as far as I am concerned, this is mainly a deckbuilding game with some strategic manoeuvering and lots and lots of theme. During the course of the game, you really need to adapt your deck to your changing needs, or else you will not be able to achieve any of your goals. After our first play, my girlfriend (who normally likes much lighter games) and I discussed what had happened, and we are both anxious to test a better strategy next time. I love it when games make you want to do that. The only drawback that I can think of so far (but it is quite a big one), is that you NEED to read the FAQ and errata before your first play. Oh, and that it is hard in my case to bring such an involved two player only game to the table on a regular basis.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The main premise of this game is using deck building mechanics to simulate logistical delays in a pre-industrial war and it's simply brilliant. Being a detail heavy Martin Wallace game there is a lot to digest, but as it is card driven most of the time you only need to work with two or three options out of the 15 or so possible actions. Thankfully, once you play a turn or two it all becomes pretty intuitive.

It's too bad the Halifax Hammer debacle derailed this one. I don't doubt it exists, but it is not something you are going to run into until you've played it 10 or 20 times, if you figure it out at all. Without that controversy, I think this might have started a whole new sub-genre of war game, as happened with the COIN System. There have been a couple, but it just hasn't taken off, which is too bad because the system is really clever.

I'll keep my fingers crossed that the new "Hands in the Sea" mod will get this ball rolling again.

RATING: 5
This game and Quarriors are proof that my negative response to the deck building genre is mostly unwarranted.  I'll probably never care for noninteractive deck-building, but deck-building tied to strategic boardplay (settling, raiding) and combat seems to be right up my alley. 

I'm not sure how I'll react if future games of Acres end up being mostly double solitaire, but I suppose that's up to me and my opponent.  And since I'll always be one of the two players in the game, that's highly unlikely.

[i]Update:[/i]

Over the past few years, I've had a reversal of opinion on deck-building.  I still dislike Dominion and its dry cousins, but I now enjoy thematic deck-builders such as Legendary, Hogwarts Battle, and Attack on Titan.  The co-op versions are particularly fun.

That opinion has not carried over to AFAoS, unfortunately. There are multiple features of the Acres system that are dull and frustrating.  Too much Euro and not enough War.  Card-driven board expansion isn't very interesting, nor is the development of towns into cities.  Nor is staring at your hand when you don't have much to do.

And let's not even get into the broken Hammer strategy, which I've avoided learning anything about. But the fact that it exists (still) warrants a ratings nosedive.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This is the most played game in my collection (counting Yucata). I love this game; it's frustrating in all the right ways. Securing VPs comes at the cost of fattening your deck and thereby makes the player in the lead increasingly vulnerable. For this reason the game always feels tight, and despite what people say about the Halifax Hammer, we've seldom used it. It either doesn't seem the best play or fails outright, and while we aren't the best AFAoS players in the world, we certainly aren't bad either. In fact, I think only one of our games has ended with the fall of Quebec. 

We've logged incredible come from behind wins and several games that came down to the wire. I usually want to play again immediately.

I'd say this game is good for at least 30 plays. I still want to play it, but as you learn the game beyond this point the joy of discovery ebbs and the emphasis shifts more to precise strategic play. The latter can be both exciting and exhausting, depending on how often you do it.

I'm very excited to see where designers take the card-drafting + area control mechanic. This is probably my favorite style of game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[b]Hawkeye:[/b]


1. Terrific art.
2. Unusual game setting. The narrative depicts the bloodthirsty war raging between England and France in the American colonies during the 17th and 18th centuries. Brings to mind that excellent Michael Mann film, [i]Last of the Mohicans[/i].
3. Huge spread of strategy and tactics available.
4. Game has depth. Planning ahead is essential and encouraged.
5. Uses deck building and drafting in the most innovative way yet devised. Certainly a good son of Dominion.
6. Gameplay forms interesting narratives, surprisingly. A lot of laughs and fun can be gleamed from ongoing sieges, and the semi-constant guerilla warfare of raiding/looting Indians. You will find yourself talking about the game when you're done - a vigorous post-mortem of the game's events is an entertaining affair here.
7. HUGE replayability.
8. The deck building engine fits the theme like a glove, and it is here that [i]A Few Acres of Snow[/i] blasts [i]Dominion[/i] down with a Pennsylvania long rifle. There is a reason (and a great one) for the buying of cards and having them take a few turns to get back to your hand: during this historical period, supplies took months to reach where they were needed, the logistics of the time were limited; sailing ships and canoes were the order of the day. I found all the game mechanisms to fit the theme's period.
9. There's a lot more game here than the rules, cards, and components indicate. Shocking how many people rate a game from a reading of the rules. This game needs you to play and explore its intricacies. You will be rewarded.
10. Having had a few hours to think about [i]A Few Acres of Snow[/i], and nothing much else besides this morning, I truly feel that studying this game's composition is akin (at least for me) to prising open the gold lid of a Constantinople-made pocket watch, and then marvelling at the precision of the whirling, silent instrumentation within.
10. I haven't played a Martin Wallace game before, but I can say that this game is a spellbinding accomplishment. Other than [i]Waterloo[/i] and [i]Moongha Invaders[/i] (which I can't find copies of), the rest of Wallace's portfolio does not particularly appeal to me, because I find the themes he chooses as dull as dishwater, generally.
11. Assymetrical design. Also, each faction seems to possess a counter-strategym for each emerging game-conquering strategy.
12. Oodles of room for player dynamism.


[b]Chingachgook:[/b]


1. Time-sucking gameplay. Takes longer to play than the rules and cards would suggest - easily 90 minutes.
2. Somewhat daunting for a new player, with an initially bewildering array of turn options.
3. The player aids are filled with gumph, but told me nothing that I really wanted to know whilst enjoying the game. Same as every other game's player-aids then.
4, The siege rules could have been a teeny bit clearer. I would have appreciated a little bold text saying, common game mistakes, and that section then clarifying the siege rules - which are good, but play out similarly to Knizia's [i]Scarab Lords.[/i] By that I mean, they read okay, and play out well, when you fully understand them: YOU check to see if YOU meet the victory conditions, at the start of your turn.
5. Steep learning curve.

Q: [i]Why are there so few wagons/roads in this game?[/i]
A: In the wilderness, there were few bridges, many rivers, and fewer roads. Any "roads" were in name only. In actuality they were little more than crude trails carved out of the backcountry, filled with stumps and sinkholes. Travelling in America was difficult if not impossible to say the least. It was the call for a 'national road' in the 1740s that would be the catalyst for the building of any good roads - in fact, there were cries for decent roads before there was even a nation.

Batteaux, canoes and rafts where mainly used in the wilderness or undeveloped areas of the world of the time. For example in the areas covered by the game, the Natives used birch bark canoes for transportation on the major rivers. The fur traders used canoes, Batteaux, and rafts to transport their yearly take of furs to outposts for transport to market.

The Batteaux's shallow draft worked well in rivers while its flat bottom profile allowed heavy loading of cargoes and provided stability. The Colonial French and British militaries sometimes fitted the larger flat-bottomed boats with small cannon - also, many of the boats could be strung together to form pontoon bridges.



[i]Please ignore the idiot, somewhere below this note, that rated the game a '2' - he rates 'Battleship' a '4' - that says it all. It really does. This is a truly excellent game, undeserving of such a rating.[/i]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
French-and-Indian War themed deckbuilder.  You can be French or British.  When you settle/capture a location, add the location card to your deck; pay money to add action cards to your deck.  Assault/raid/besiege your opponents' locations, trade for income, recruit soldiers, artillery, and natives, fine tune your deck, capture the most victory points (locations and opponents' pieces).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm really not sure how I feel about this yet.  I like a lot of the mechanisms, and I love the way acquiring a location provides you with resources, but increases your logistical difficulties.  It feels right; it makes sense.  What I dislike is that you can founder pretty thoroughly if you get inundated with "the wrong cards".  Unlike the [family=50][/family] games, there usually are useful things to do with the cards, unless you've snagged a bunch of locations willy-nilly, with no regard for their function.  Certainly, knowing the environment of the conflict enables you to dominate the situation over someone who doesn't.

Oh, and I also adore that the sieges are vaguely like [thing=9446][/thing], rather than anything involving dice.  You can still score a lucky win, but it's more likely if you've managed your deck (your attention to that which you rule) well.
